Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Retaliation under Duress: Coercer Only
Determines that only the coercer is subject to qisas for killing under duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qisas under duress, explain the instrument (āla) analogy, and distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from Zufar's.

Core Idea
Under duress, the coercer bears qisas; the coerced is an instrument.

When a person is forced to kill, who should be executed—the one who physically commits the murder or the one who compelled him? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, holds that only the coercer is liable for retaliation (qisas). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, including the instrument analogy and an important intra-madhhab disagreement with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Ruling
Locate the matn statement: 'ويقادُ المَكْرِه فقط' – only the coercer is subject to qisas. This is the authoritative position in the Hanafi school.
The matn is the base text; the commentary expands on its rationale.
2
Understand the Instrument (āla) Analogy
The coerced is likened to a sword or fire—an instrument that acts according to its nature. Under duress, the coerced's natural inclination to preserve life makes him a passive tool of the coercer.
The analogy explains why liability shifts to the coercer.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Imam Zufar disagrees, arguing that the coerced is the direct agent (mubashir) and thus should bear qisas. Contrast this with the majority view.
This ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school, not cross-madhhab.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to Zufar
The Hanafi response: for worldly liability (qisas), the act is attributed to the coercer; for sin, it remains on the coerced because religious accountability (dīn) cannot be transferred via instrumentality.
This dual attribution preserves both justice and moral responsibility.
5
Apply Edge Cases
If the coercer is a minor or insane, no qisas is due on anyone because the coercer is not legally accountable. The act is attributed to the minor/madman, but they are not liable for qisas.
This limitation shows that the coercer must be legally responsible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقادُ المَكْرِه فقط. [4] قوله : ويقاد المكره فقط ؛ قال في «النهاية»: سواء كان المكره الأمر يانعاً عاقلاً أو معتوهاً أو غلاماً غير يانع ، فالقود على الآمر، ونسبه إلى «المبسوط»، فنسب الشيخ الأكمل عبد العزيز الله قول صاحب «النهاية» إلى السهو. وقال : الرواية في «المبسوط» بفتح الراء دون كسرها، وروى عن أبي اليسر له في مبسوطه»: ولو كان الأمر صبياً أو مجنوناً لم يجب القصاص على أحد؛ لأنّ القائل في الحقيقة هذا الصبي والمجنون، وهما ليسا بأهل لوجوب العقوبة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي) ناقلاً. [1]قوله : فعند أبي حنيفة ... الخ ؛ القصاص على الحامل، وهو المكره لأنّ الفاعل ... الخ ؛ توضيحه : إنّ المكرَه محمول على القتل بطبعه إيثارا لحياته فيصير آلة للمكره بالكسر فيما يصلح آلة له، وهو القتل، بأن يلقيه عليه. فإن قيل : لو كان المكره آلة لأضيف الإثم إليه، كالقتل. قلت : لا يصلح المكرة آلة له في الجناية على دينه، فبقي الفعل مقصوراً عليه في حق الأثم، كما تقولُ في الإكراه على الإعتاق. هذا ما يستفاد من «الهداية». [2]قوله : يصير آلة له ؛ وذلك لأنّ الآلة هي التي تعمل بطبعها كالسيف، فإن طبعه القتلُ عند الاستعمال في محلّه، وكالنار فإنّ طبعها الإحراق، وكالماء فإنّ طبعه الإغراق ، وإذا كان كذلك ففي الجري على موجب الطبع مشابهة بالآلة. ولو استعمل القاتل آلته التي هي السيف في شخص ظلماً فقتله يجب القصاص على القاتل، فكذا يجب القصاص على المكرَه هاهنا ؛ لكون المكرة آلة له. كذا في «الكفاية». [3] قوله : لأنه مباشر...الخ ؛ وهو ظاهر؛ لأنّ الفعل من المكره حقيقة؛ لصدوره منه بغير واسط، وحسّاً؛ لأنه معاين ومشاهد عنه، وكذا شرعاً؛ لأنه قرر الشرع حكمه
And only the coercer is subject to retaliation. [4] His statement: 'and only the coercer is subject to retaliation'; it is said in al-Nihayah: whether the coercer is mature, sane, an idiot, or a minor not mature, the qisas is on the commander, and he attributed this to al-Mabsut. Then the perfect scholar Abd al-Aziz Allah attributed the statement of the author of al-Nihayah to oversight. He said: the narration in al-Mabsut has a fatha on the ra' (mukrah) not a kasra, and it is narrated from Abi al-Yusr in his Mabsut: if the commander was a boy or a madman, qisas is obligatory on no one, because the real perpetrator is the boy or madman, and they are not liable for punishment. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting. [1] His statement: 'According to Abu Hanifa... etc.'; qisas is on the one who compels (al-hamil), i.e., the coercer (mukri), because the agent... etc.; explanation: the coerced (mukrah) is naturally driven to kill out of love for his life, so he becomes an instrument for the coercer (mukri) in what is suitable as an instrument for him, which is killing, by being thrown upon [the victim]. If it is said: if the coerced were an instrument, then the sin would be attributed to him, like killing. I say: the coerced is not an instrument for the coercer in the crime against his religion, so the act is confined to him regarding sin, as you say in coercion to manumit. This is derived from al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'becomes an instrument for him'; that is because the instrument is that which works by its nature, like the sword, whose nature is to kill when used on its target, and fire, whose nature is to burn, and water, whose nature is to drown. When this is the case, following the nature resembles the instrument. And if a killer uses his instrument, the sword, on a person unjustly and kills him, qisas is obligatory on the killer. So likewise qisas is obligatory on the coerced here because the coercer is an instrument for him. Thus in al-Kifayah. [3] His statement: 'Because he is the direct agent... etc.'; it is apparent because the act of the coerced is real, occurring directly from him without intermediary, and sensory, as it is observed and witnessed from him, and likewise legally, because the law has established its ruling.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage addresses retaliation (qisas) when a person is coerced into killing. The matn states that only the coercer (mukri) is subject to qisas, not the coerced (mukrah). The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that qisas is on the coercer because the coerced acts as an instrument (āla) of the coercer, akin to a sword in the hand of a killer. Zufar, however, holds qisas on the coerced because he is the direct agent. The Hanafi position is that the coerced's act is attributed to the coercer for liability, but for sin, the act remains on the coerced because the coerced cannot be an instrument for the coercer in matters of faith (dīn). The commentary also discusses edge cases: if the commander is a minor or insane, no qisas is due because they are not legally responsible. The principle is that the coercer's liability stems from the coerced being an instrument, but the instrumentality does not transfer sin, only worldly liability.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for murder or injury; here specifically capital punishment for intentional killing.
آلة (āla): Instrument; the coerced person is considered a tool of the coercer for the purpose of legal liability.
مباشر (mubashir): Direct agent; the one who directly performs the act without intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, who is subject to qisas when a person is forced to kill under duress?
A) The coerced person
B) The coercer
C) Both
D) Neither if the coercer is a minor

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, only the coercer is subject to qisas for a killing committed under duress; the coerced is treated as an instrument.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Qisas Liability in Coerced Killing: The Hanafi View
Explaining the Hanafi position and disputes on qisas for coerced killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that qisas falls on the coercer in coerced killing, distinguish it from the views of Zufar, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i, and explain the instrument analogy used as proof.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the coercer bears qisas because the coerced is merely an instrument—like a sword.

When a person is forced under threat to kill another, who is liable for qisas? The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, holds that the coercer (al-hamil) is liable because the coerced acts as his instrument. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this position and contrasts it with the views of other Hanafi disciples and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: qisas is on the coercer (al-hamil) because the coerced (al-mukrah) becomes an instrument.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Zufar: qisas on the direct killer (mubashir). Abu Yusuf: no qisas due to shubha. Al-Shafi'i: both are liable.
Zufar is a Hanafi disciple; his view is an intra-madhhab difference.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is qiyas: the coerced is like a sword or fire—an instrument that acts by nature. The coercer, like the user of the sword, is liable.
This is qiyas khafi (concealed analogy).
4
Examine the Hanafi response to objections
The text addresses a potential objection: if the coerced is an instrument, why is sin on him? The response: sin remains on the coerced because he chose life over death, but qisas is a worldly matter attributed to the coercer.
This shows the distinction between worldly liability (qisas) and spiritual responsibility (ithm).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi: coercer executed; Zufar: coerced executed; Shafi'i: both executed if conditions met; Abu Yusuf: no execution.
This thamarah shows the real-world impact of the legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه
(٢) فعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد القصاص على الحامل؛ لأن الفاعل يصير آلة له، وعند زفر القصاص على الفاعل؛ لأنه مباشر، ولا يحل له القتل. الأكمل عبد العزيز الله قول صاحب «النهاية» إلى السهو. وقال: الرواية في «المبسوط» بفتح الراء دون كسرها، وروى عن أبي اليسر له في مبسوطه: ولو كان الأمر صبياً أو مجنوناً لم يجب القصاص على أحد؛ لأن القائل في الحقيقة هذا الصبي والمجنون، وهما ليسا بأهل لوجوب العقوبة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلاً عن «العناية». [١] قوله: فعند أبي حنيفة... الخ؛ القصاص على الحامل، وهو المكره لأن الفاعل... الخ؛ توضيحه: إن المكرَه محمول على القتل بطبعه إيثارا لحياته فيصير آلة للمكره بالكسر فيما يصلح آلة له، وهو القتل، بأن يلقيه عليه. فإن قيل: لو كان المكره آلة لأضيف الإثم إليه، كالقتل. قلت: لا يصلح المكرة آلة له في الجناية على دينه، فبقي الفعل مقصوراً عليه في حق الأثم، كما تقول في الإكراه على الإعتاق. هذا ما يستفاد من «الهداية». [٢] قوله: يصير آلة له؛ وذلك لأن الآلة هي التي تعمل بطبعها كالسيف، فإن طبعه القتل عند الاستعمال في محله، وكالنار فإن طبعها الإحراق، وكالماء فإن طبعه الإغراق، وإذا كان كذلك ففي الجري على موجب الطبع مشابهة بالآلة. ولو استعمل القاتل آلته التي هي السيف في شخص ظلماً فقتله يجب القصاص على القاتل، فكذا يجب القصاص على المكرَه هاهنا؛ لكون المكرة آلة له. كذا في «الكفاية». [٣] قوله: لأنه مباشر... الخ؛ وهو ظاهر؛ لأن الفعل من المكره حقيقة؛ لصدوره منه بغير واسطة، وحساً؛ لأنه معاين ومشاهد عنه، وكذا شرعاً؛ لأنه قرر الشرع حكمه وعند أبي يوسف لا يجب على أحد للشبهة، وعند الشافعي يجب عليهما على الفاعل بالمباشرة، وعلى الحامل بالتسبيب، والتسبيب عنده كالمباشرة: كشهود القصاص.
The Book of Coercion. (2) According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the qisas is upon the coercer because the doer becomes an instrument for him. According to Zufar, qisas is upon the doer because he is direct, and it is not permissible for him to kill. Al-Akmal Abd al-Aziz [said] the statement of the author of al-Nihaya is an oversight. He said: the narration in al-Mabsut is with fatha on the ra without kasra, and it is narrated from Abu al-Yusr in his Mabsut: If the one who commands is a child or insane, qisas is not obligatory upon anyone because the speaker in reality is the child or insane, and they are not qualified for punishment. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Inaya. [1] His statement: 'According to Abu Hanifa... etc'; qisas is upon the coercer, who is the one who compels, because the doer... etc; its clarification: The coerced one is compelled to kill by his nature, preferring his life, so he becomes an instrument for the coercer in what is suitable as an instrument, which is killing, by throwing him upon him. If it is said: If the coerced one were an instrument, sin would be attributed to him, like the killing. I say: The coerced one is not suitable as an instrument for him in crimes against his religion, so the act remains confined to him regarding sin, as you say in coercion to manumit. This is derived from al-Hidaya. [2] His statement: 'becomes an instrument for him'; that is because an instrument is that which works by its nature, like the sword, for its nature is killing when used in its place, and like fire, for its nature is burning, and like water, for its nature is drowning. If that is so, then acting according to the nature resembles an instrument. If the killer uses his instrument which is the sword on a person unjustly and kills him, qisas is obligatory upon the killer. Likewise, qisas is obligatory upon the coercer here because the coerced one is an instrument for him. Thus in al-Kifaya. [3] His statement: 'because he is direct... etc'; it is apparent because the act from the coerced one is real, as it originates from him without intermediary, and sensory, because it is witnessed and observed from him, and likewise legally because the law has established its ruling. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not obligatory upon anyone due to ambiguity. According to al-Shafi'i, it is obligatory upon both: upon the doer for direct action, and upon the coercer for causation, and causation with him is like direct action: like the witnesses of qisas.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding qisas (retaliation) when a person is coerced to kill. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: qisas is on the coercer (al-hamil), because the coerced killer (al-mukrah) becomes an instrument (ala) of the coercer, similar to a sword or fire. The act is attributed to the coercer for the purpose of qisas, though sin remains on the coerced because he chose to preserve his own life. The commentator then clarifies that Zufar holds qisas on the direct killer because he is the immediate agent. A footnote addresses a case where the commanding person is a child or insane: no qisas is due on anyone because the effective speaker is the child/insane, who lacks capacity. The commentary then explains the instrument analogy in detail: just as using a sword makes the wielder liable, so the coercer is liable because the coerced acts as his instrument. It then mentions Abu Yusuf's view that no qisas is due due to a legal ambiguity (shubha), and al-Shafi'i's position that both parties are liable: the doer for direct killing and the coercer for causation, which Shafi'i treats as equivalent to direct action, citing the analogy of false witnesses in a qisas case. The Hanafi position is that the coerced is an instrument in the physical act but not in the sin, so qisas is on the coercer.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will under threat of harm.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for intentional killing or injury.
الحامل (al-hamil): The person who compels another to commit an act (the coercer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The coercer is the one who intended the killing
B) The coerced becomes an instrument of the coercer, like a sword
C) The coerced is excused due to insanity
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school places qisas on the coercer in coerced killing, treating the coerced as an instrument, while other schools assign liability differently based on directness, shubha, or dual liability.

Introduction to the Book of Jinayat: Placement and Definitions
Explains placement of jinayat and defines key terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reason for placing the Book of Jinayat after Rahn and define 'jinaya' linguistically and legally according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Jinayat protects life just as Rahn protects wealth; means precede ends.

This lesson explores the opening of the Book of Jinayat, where the commentator justifies its placement after the Book of Rahn and clarifies the meaning of 'jinaya'. Understanding this placement reveals an important methodological principle: means precede ends. You will also learn the technical term 'jinaya' and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the placement argument
Read the passage to find why the Book of Jinayat is placed after Rahn. Note the comparison: each protects something – wealth vs. soul.
2
Analyze the rebuttal
Find the objection from Ghayat al-Bayan and the commentator's response. Understand why the objection is considered weak.
The objection claims rahn is legislated while jinaya is forbidden; the response focuses on the rulings, not the acts.
3
Deconstruct the definition
Extract the linguistic and legal meanings of 'jinaya'. Note that linguistically it means 'earning' and legally it is a forbidden act affecting soul or limbs.
4
Recognize the seven aspects
List the seven aspects for discussing jinayat: legislativity, cause of obligation, linguistic meaning, legal meaning, pillar, condition, ruling.
This framework will guide the detailed study of jinayat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات كتاب الجنايات)
١ ] قوله : كتاب الجنايات ؛ أورد الجنايات بعد الرّهن ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما للوقاية والصيانة، فإنّ الرّهن وثيقة لصيانة المال، وحكمُ الجناية لصيانة النفس، ألا ترى إلى قوله : وَلَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَوةٌ يَتأُولِي الْأَلْبَابِ ، ولَمَّا كان المالُ وسيلة لبقاء النفس ، قَدَّمَ الرَّهن على الجنايات، بناءً على تقدم الوسائل على المقاصد، كذا في أكثر الشروح.
(1)
وما قال في «غاية «البيان»: ولكن قَدَّمَ الرّهنَ ؛ لأنه مشروع بالكتاب والسنة بخلاف الجناية؛ لأنها محظور، فإنّها عبارة عمّا ليس للإنسان فعله ؛ ليس بشيء؛ لأنّ المقصود بالبيان في كتاب الجنايات إنّما هو أحكام الجنايات دون أنفسها، ولا شك أنّ أحكامها أيضاً مشروعة وثابتة بالكتاب والسنة.
وأيضاً لا معنى لتأخيرها من هذه الحيثية، ثمّ إنّ الجناية في اللغة اسم لما تجنيه من شيء؛ أي تكسبه، وهي في الأصل مصدر جنى عليه شر جناية، وهو عام في كل ما يقبح بسوء إلا أنه في الشرع خُص بفعل محرم حل بالنفس والأطراف، والأوّل يُسمى قتلاً، وهو فعل من العباد تزولُ به الحياة، والثاني يسمى قطعاً وجرحاً، وفي الشروح :
الكلام في الجنايات من أوجه :
الأوَّلُ : في معرفة مشروعيتها.
والثاني : في سبب وجوبها.
والثالث : في تفسيرها لغة. والرابع : في تفسيرها عند الفقهاء.
والخامس: في ركنها.
والسادس: في شرطها. والسابع : في حكمها.
(۱) البقرة : ۱۷۹.
Book of Crimes: [He] said: 'Book of Crimes'; he brought the Crimes after the Pledge because each one of them is for protection and preservation. Indeed, the pledge is a document for the preservation of wealth, and the ruling of crime is for the preservation of the soul. Do you not see His statement: 'And in retaliation there is life, O people of understanding.' And since wealth is a means for the preservation of the soul, he placed the Pledge before Crimes, based on the precedence of means over ends. Thus in most commentaries. And what is said in 'Ghayat al-Bayan': 'But he placed the Pledge because it is legislated by the Book and Sunnah contrary to crime because it is forbidden, for it is an expression of what man is not allowed to do' is nothing; because the purpose of explanation in the Book of Crimes is only the rulings of crimes, not the crimes themselves, and there is no doubt that their rulings are also legislated and established by the Book and Sunnah. Also, there is no meaning to delaying it from this perspective. Then, 'al-jinaya' in language is a name for what one earns from something, i.e., acquires, and it is originally a verbal noun: he committed an evil crime against him, and it is general in everything that is ugly with evil, except that in the Sharia it is specified to a forbidden act that affects the soul and limbs. The first is called killing, which is an act of the servants by which life ceases, and the second is called cutting and wounding. In the commentaries: Discussion on crimes from aspects: First: in knowing its legislativity. Second: in the cause of its obligation. Third: in its linguistic interpretation. Fourth: in its interpretation among jurists. Fifth: in its pillar. Sixth: in its condition. Seventh: in its ruling. (1) Al-Baqara: 179.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a prefatory commentary from the sharh on the matn of Al-Hidayah. It opens with the reason for placing the Book of Crimes (Jinayat) after the Book of Pledge (Rahn). The commentator argues that both deal with protection: rahn protects wealth, while jinayat protects life. He cites the Quranic verse on qisas (2:179) as proof that qisas preserves life. Then he refutes an objection from 'Ghayat al-Bayan' that rahn is legislated by primary sources while jinayat is inherently forbidden and thus should not be compared. The commentator responds that the book's subject is the rulings of jinayat, not the acts themselves, and these rulings are legislated by Quran and Sunnah. Next, he defines 'jinaya' linguistically as earning or acquiring something, and legally as a prohibited act affecting the soul or limbs. Killing is one type; wounding another. Finally, he lists seven aspects of discussion: (1) knowledge of its legislativity, (2) cause of obligation, (3) linguistic meaning, (4) legal meaning, (5) pillar (rukn), (6) condition (shart), and (7) ruling (hukm). This sets the stage for the detailed treatment of jinayat.

Key Terms
الجناية (al-jināyah): In this text, it is defined linguistically as 'what one earns or acquires' and legally as 'a forbidden act that affects the soul and limbs of a person.' It includes killing and wounding.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation, specifically the legal penalty of taking life for life or limb for limb.

Intentional Killing: Definition, Proof, and Ruling
Defines intentional killing and proves the obligation of qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define intentional killing, cite its Quranic and Hadith proofs, state the ruling (qisas/diya/sin), and identify the condition of equivalence.

Core Idea
Intentional killing requires qisas unless pardoned; equivalence between killer and victim is a condition.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya introduces the five types of killing, focusing on the first: intentional killing (qatl al-'amd). The commentator defines it legally, provides the scriptural proof for qisas, and outlines its pillar, condition, and possible outcomes. Understanding these elements is essential for applying Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types of killing
Note the classification: 'amd, shibh 'amd, khata', jari majra al-khata', and qatl sabab. This sets the context for the first type.
The footnote clarifies that these five types entail sin, qisas, etc., excluding lawful killings like qisas punishment.
2
Locate the definition of intentional killing
Find the legal definition (shar'an): an act prohibited by Sharia that ends life. Distinguish it from crimes against property (ghasb) and hunting in ihram.
The linguistic definition is mentioned as previously given.
3
Examine the proof for qisas
The Quranic verse (2:178) 'kutiba 'alaykumu al-qisas' and the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad'. In Hanafi usul, the imperative 'kutiba' indicates obligation (wujub).
The hadith is from Darqutni and Ibn Abi Shayba.
4
Identify the pillar and condition
The pillar (rukn) is the killing act itself. The condition (shart) for qisas is equivalence (mumathala) between the killer and victim.
Equivalence includes legal responsibility and protected blood status.
5
State the ruling
The ruling is obligation of qisas, diya, or sin depending on circumstances (e.g., pardon, lack of equivalence).
The footnote excludes other types of lawful killing that do not carry these consequences.

Book of Crimes / Types of Killing and What Necessitates Qisas and Testimony Upon It
Intentional Killing
425
Know that killing is of five types: intentional, quasi-intentional, erroneous, that which runs in the course of error, and killing by cause. He explained these types with their rulings and said: Intentional Killing. As for the first, it is knowledge of its legislativity due to His statement: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is qisas' and his statement: 'Intentional killing requires qisas, and killing is aggression.' The cause of the legislativity of qisas is the removal of corruption from the earth. As for its linguistic meaning, it has been mentioned previously. As for its meaning in the Sharia, it is a name for an act prohibited legally, whether concerning wealth or life, but in the custom of jurists, when used absolutely, it refers to the name of the crime occurring against the soul and limbs of a human being. The crime occurring against wealth is called usurpation, and the crime occurring from a person in ihram or in the sanctuary against game is the crime of the muhrim. As for its pillar, it is killing, which is an act attributed to the servants by which life ceases by mere habit. As for its condition, it is equivalence and equality in exacting retribution, because equivalence is a condition for the validity of penalties and guarantee of aggressions. As for its ruling, it is the obligation of qisas or diya or sin. This is the summary of what is in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'. (1) His statement: 'Killing is of five types' means that which is confined here is what when found, some of the mentioned rulings such as sin and qisas etc. are attached to it. So it repels what was suspected that the enumeration is impossible because there are five other types such as killing by qisas, stoning for zina, crucifixion for highway robbery, killing the apostate, and killing the combatant; because none of these has any of those rulings attached to them. And to this the author of 'Al-Hidayah' alluded with his statement.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's classification of killing into five types, focusing on the first: intentional killing (qatl al-'amd). The commentator first states the five types: 'amd, shibh 'amd, khata', jari majra al-khata', and qatl sabab. Then he explains the first type. He provides the evidence for the legislativity of qisas: the Quranic verse (2:178) and the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' (intentional killing requires qisas). The cause for qisas is to remove corruption. He then defines 'amd linguistically (already given) and legally: it is a prohibited act affecting the soul or limbs. He distinguishes it from crimes against property (ghasb) and against game in ihram. The pillar (rukn) of 'amd is the killing act itself that ends life. The condition for qisas is equivalence (mumathala) between the victim and the perpetrator, meaning the killer must be legally responsible and the victim protected. The ruling is either qisas (retaliation) or diya (blood money) or sin (ithm), depending on circumstances such as pardon or conditions. The footnote clarifies that these five types are those that result in sin, qisas, etc., excluding other types of lawful killing (e.g., qisas punishment, stoning, etc.).

Key Terms
القتل العمد (al-qatl al-'amd): Intentional killing: an act that ends life with deliberate intent, which is a prohibited act affecting the soul.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation, the legal penalty of taking life for life or limb for limb.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money, a financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs in lieu of qisas.

Key Takeaway
Intentional killing is a prohibited act that obligates qisas, diya, or sin, with equivalence as a key condition.

Qisas — Retaliation

Intentional Killing (ʿAmd) and the Instrument for Qisas: Hanafi Positions
Explaining the definition of 'amd and the condition of the instrument for qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of intentional killing ('amd) for qisas and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding the qualifying instrument.

Core Idea
Intentional killing (ʿamd) requires a sharp instrument according to Abu Hanifa, but any instrument the body cannot bear suffices according to the Sahibayn.

In Hanafi criminal law, qisas (retaliation) for murder depends on whether the killing is classified as 'amd (intentional). This classification hinges not only on the killer's intention but also on the instrument used. The classical Hanafi masters disagreed: Abu Hanifa restricted 'amd to sharp, dismembering instruments, while his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, extended it to any instrument the victim's body cannot withstand. This lesson unpacks that difference, its legal consequences, and the Quranic evidence supporting each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'amd from the text
Locate the condition 'something that separates the parts' in the Arabic passage. Note that the strike must be intentional and the instrument must have a disintegrating effect.
The matn uses 'ma yufarriq al-ajza' (separates the parts).
2
List instruments considered 'amd according to Abu Hanifa
From the text, Abu Hanifa requires the instrument to be sharp (muhaddad) like a weapon, knife, sharp wood/stone, reed, or fire. Anything without sharpness (e.g., flat side of iron, blunt object) is not 'amd for qisas.
Fire is included because it splits the skin like iron.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani define 'amd as striking with something the body cannot bear, such as a huge stone or large log. This does not require sharpness.
Al-Shafi'i holds a similar view.
4
Examine the legal consequence and the mufta bihi
Under Abu Hanifa, killing with a blunt rock incurs only diyah and kaffarah, not qisas. Under the Sahibayn, qisas is wajib. The mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) is the Sahibayn's view.
Text notes: 'عليه الفتوى' though not explicit in this passage; later scholars like al-Marghinani adopt the Sahibayn's view.
5
Review the Quranic evidence and its application
Quran 4:93 establishes eternal punishment for intentional killing, and 2:178 commands qisas. The hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' specifies that only 'amd triggers qisas. The text extends sin to killing dhimmis via qiyas on equal inviolability.
Dalala of the verses is zanni, but thubut is qati'.

[1] قوله: ضربة؛ من قبيل ذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب؛ أي إرهاق الروح بالضرب القصدي وإلا فلا شك في أن الضرب ليس عين القتل عمداً.
[2] قوله: كسلاح ومحدد ... الخ؛ السلاح بكسر السين المهملة ما أعد للحرب من آلة الحديد، كذا في «شرح المشارق»، والمحدّد: اسم مفعول من حدد السيف؛ أي جعله حاداً أو حديداً بمعنى قاطعاً بسرعة.

وبه يأثم، ويجب القود عيناً هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما وعند الشافعي: ضربه قصداً بما لا تطيقه البنية، حتى إن ضربه بحجر عظيم أو خشب عظيم، فهو عمد، وبه يأثم ويجب القود عيناً، هذا عندنا خلافاً للشافعي.
[1] قوله: وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، أي اشتراط الحدة بمعنى أن القتل بما ليس له حدة كشيء يقتل، أو صفحة حديد أو نحاس، لا يوجب القصاص عنده، وقد ذكر قاضي خان: إن الجرح لا يشترط في الحديد وما يشبهه؛ كالنحاس وغيره في ظاهر الرواية. كذا في «التبيين».
[2] قوله: به يأثم؛ لقوله: {وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُّتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَالِدًا فِيهَا}، وقد نطق به كثير من السنة، وعليه انعقد الإجماع، والآية المذكورة وإن أفادت الإثم في قتل المؤمن متعمداً فقط بعبارة النص إلا أنها تفيد المأثم في قتل الذمي عمداً أيضاً بدلالة النص، بناءً على ثبوت المساواة في العصمة بين المسلم والذمي نظراً إلى التكليف أو الدار.
[3] قوله: ويجب القود؛ لقوله: {كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى}، والآية بظاهرها لم تفصل بين العمد والخطأ، لكنها تقيد بوصف العمدية لقوله: «العمد قود»؛ ولأن الجناية بها تتكامل، وحكمة الزجر تتوفر، والعقوبة المتناهية لا شرع لها دون ذلك. القود - بفتحتين -: القصاص.
[4] قوله: عيناً؛ أي ليس حق المولى إلا فيه، وليس له أخذ الدية من الجاني إلا برضاه. هذا خلاصة ما في شروح «الهداية».
He struck him intentionally with something that separates the parts, like a weapon or a sharp object made of wood or stone or a reed or fire. Strike intentionally with something that separates the parts, like a weapon and a sharp object from wood or stone or a reed or fire. (And the intent is to explain the killing to which rulings are attached; this is what my brother Jilabi indicated).
[1] His saying: 'a strike'; it is of the type of mentioning the cause and intending the effect; i.e., bringing the soul to exhaustion by intentional striking, otherwise there is no doubt that striking is not the same as intentional killing.
[2] His saying: 'like a weapon and a sharp object, etc.'; the weapon (silah) with a kasra on the sin is what is prepared for war from iron tools, as in 'Sharh al-Mashariq', and al-muhaddad is a passive participle from 'he sharpened the sword'; i.e., he made it sharp or cutting quickly.
[3] His saying: 'and a reed (laytah)'; with a kasra on the lam and sukun on the ya and a detached ta', it is the peel of the reed, and in 'al-Mughrib': thus it is permissible to slaughter with the reed.
[4] His saying: 'and fire'; I say: what occurs to sound minds is that it is conjuncted to 'muhaddad' because fire is among the disintegrating [agents], as al-Itqani stated by his saying: 'And whatever is not of the category of iron but does its work, then it is intentional, such as when he burns him with fire, then it is intentional that necessitates qisas, because it splits the skin.' And he said in 'al-Kifayah 'ala al-Hidayah': Do you not see that it does the work of iron, such that when placed at the throat and cuts what must be cut in slaughter, and blood flows, it becomes permissible; and if it cauterizes and blood does not flow, it is not permissible. Based on this, if the word 'nar' occurs with a waw (and) not a ya (my fire), as in most copies, it would be more apparent.
And by it he is sinful, and qisas is obligated absolutely: this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [companions] and according to al-Shafi'i: He struck him intentionally with something the body cannot bear, such that if he strikes him with a huge stone or huge wood, then it is intentional, and by it he is sinful, and qisas is obligated absolutely. This is according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i.
[1] His saying: 'And this is according to Abu Hanifa', i.e., the condition of sharpness, meaning that killing with something that has no sharpness like something that kills, or the flat side of iron or copper, does not necessitate qisas according to him. Qadi Khan mentioned: Wounding is not required for iron and what resembles it, like copper and others, in the apparent narration. As in 'al-Tabyin'.
[2] His saying: 'by it he is sinful'; due to His saying: {And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein}, and much of the Sunnah has stated it, and consensus has been established upon it. And the mentioned verse, although it only conveys sin in killing a believer intentionally by the text's wording, it also conveys sin in killing a dhimmi intentionally by indication of the text, based on the establishment of equality in inviolability between a Muslim and a dhimmi considering obligation or abode.
[3] His saying: 'and qisas is obligated'; due to His saying: {Retaliation is prescribed for you in the case of killing}, and the verse in its apparent meaning does not differentiate between intentional and mistake, but it is qualified by the description of intent due to his saying: 'Intentional killing necessitates qisas'; and because the crime becomes complete with it, and the wisdom of deterrence is fulfilled, and the final punishment has no share without that. Al-qawd (with two fatha) is qisas.
[4] His saying: 'absolutely'; i.e., the right of the guardian is only in it, and he does not have the right to take blood money from the offender except with his consent. This is the summary of what is in the commentaries of 'al-Hidayah'.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of intentional killing ('amd) in Hanafi fiqh, specifically which instruments qualify for qisas. The text comes from a commentary (sharh) on Al-Wiqayah, expanding its rulings. The author first explains the matn's phrase 'struck him intentionally with something that separates the parts' as a description of causing death through intentional striking. The commentary then defines key terms: 'weapon' (silah) refers to iron tools prepared for war; 'sharp' (muhaddad) means a cut made sharp; 'reed' (laytah) is the peel of a reed, permissible for slaughter; and 'fire' (nar) is included as a disintegrating agent, treated like a sharp object because it splits the skin—similar to iron. The text then shifts to the positions on what constitutes 'amd for qisas: According to Abu Hanifa, only a sharp instrument (like a sword or knife) necessitates qisas; killing with something blunt or heavy (like a stone's flat side) does not. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two companions), and also al-Shafi'i, any instrument that the body cannot bear—such as a large stone or large wood—qualifies as 'amd requiring qisas. The commentary justifies the sinfulness of all intentional killing through Quran 4:93 and extends it to dhimmis via indication. Qisas is justified through Quran 2:178, specifying that the verse generalizes but is restricted to intent by the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' (intentional killing requires retaliation). The phrase 'absolutely' ('aynan') means the victim's guardian has no right to accept blood money (diyah) unless the offender agrees. This passage clarifies the Hanafi intra-school difference: Abu Hanifa's stricter condition versus the broader view of the two companions, which is often the mufta bihi (relied upon) in later fatwa.

Key Terms
سلاح (silah): An iron tool prepared for war, such as a sword or knife.
محدد (muhaddad): A sharp object, from 'sharpening'; something that cuts quickly.
ليطة (laytah): Reed peel; a type of plant material used as a cutting tool.
نار (nar): Fire, included as a disintegrating agent that works like a sharp instrument.
عمد ('amd): Intentional killing; specific definition varies by instrument sharpness or overwhelming force.
القود (al-qawad): Qisas, retaliation; the right of the victim's guardian to execute the killer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Intentional killing ('amd) for qisas in Hanafi fiqh requires a sharp or disintegrating instrument according to Abu Hanifa, while the relied-upon view (Sahibayn) includes any instrument the body cannot bear.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Position on Kaffarah for Intentional Murder
Inter-madhhab dispute on kaffarah for intentional murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that kaffarah is not required for intentional murder and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of shubhah.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is for sins with shubhah; intentional murder is pure sin, so kaffarah does not apply.

This passage presents a classical disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over whether expiation (kaffarah) is obligatory for intentional murder. The Hanafi school holds that kaffarah is only required for accidental killing, not intentional, based on the nature of the sin—intentional murder is a pure major sin (kabirah mahdah) with no semblance of permissibility. The Shafi'i school argues by analogy that if kaffarah is required for the lesser sin, it should be required for the greater. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply usul principles like qiyas and the role of shubhah in determining legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: kaffarah is not obligatory for intentional murder ('amd). Note the distinction from accidental killing (khatta), where kaffarah is required.
The passage explicitly negates kaffarah for 'amd: 'No expiation' (لا الكفارة).
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kaffarah is obligatory for intentional murder by way of a fortiori analogy (qiyas awla).
Shafi'i's reasoning: if it is required in accidental (lighter sin), it is even more required in intentional (heavier sin).
3
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Understand the structure of the Shafi'i argument: 'wajabat fi al-khatta, fa awla an tajiba fi al-'amd'. This is a qiyas based on the illa of need for expiation.
The Shafi'i view presumes that the need for expiation increases with the gravity of the sin.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
Hanafis reject the analogy because the nature of the sin differs: accidental killing has shubhah (semblance of permissibility), while intentional murder is a pure major sin with no shubhah. Kaffarah is attached to shubhah, not to pure sin.
Also, kaffarah is legislated for lesser offenses; the higher offense carries other penalties like inheritance deprivation.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In Hanafi fiqh, the intentional killer does not perform kaffarah but is deprived of inheritance. In Shafi'i fiqh, the killer must perform kaffarah in addition to other penalties.
This difference affects the killer's religious obligations and the estate distribution.

فإنَّ القَوَدَ غير متعين عنده ، بل الولي بخير بينَ القَوَدِ وأخذ الدّية. لنا : أن المال إنما يجب في الخطا ضرورة صيانة الدم عن الهدر، إذ لا مماثلة بينه وبين النفس ، ففي العمد لا يجب مع احتمال المثل صورة ومعنى، (لا الكفارة)، خلافاً للشافعي له، وهو يقول : وجبت في الخطأ، فأولى أن تجب في العمد، ونحن نقول : لا يلزم من كون الكفَّارة ساترة للخطأ كونها ساترة للعمد، وهو كبيرة محضة. قوله : فإن القود غير متعين ؛ يعني الواجب أحدهما أي القَودُ أو الدية لا بعينه، ويتعين باختيار الوليّ ؛ لأنّ حقَّ العبدِ شرع جابراً، وفي كل واحد نوع جبر، فيتخير بين القودِ وأخذ الدية. قوله : لنا أن المال... الخ ؛ توضيحه : إنّ المال لا يصلح موجباً لعدم المماثلة، والقصاص يصلح للتماثل، وفيه مصلحة الأحياء، زجراً وجبراً، فيتعين، وفي الخطأ : وجوب المال ضرورة صونِ الدَّمِ عن الهدر، ولا يتيقن بعدم قصد الولي بعد أخذ المال ، فلا يتعين دفعاً للهلاك. قوله : فأولى أن تجب... الخ ؛ لأنّ الحاجة إلى الكفّارة في العمد أمس منها إليه في الخطأ، فكان العمد أدعى إلى إيجابها. ولنا : إن العمد كبيرة محضة إلى حرام ليس فيه شبهة الحل، وفي الكفارة معنى العبادة، فلا تناط بمثلها ؛ ولأن الكفارة من مقادير الشرع، وتعينها في الشرع لدفع الأدنى، لا بعينها لدفع الأعلى، ومن حكمه حرمان الميراث وإن لم يصرح به، ولكن أشار بعد بقوله : ولا إرث إلا هاهنا كما سيأتي، ودليلُ الحرمان قوله : «لا ميراث لقاتل».
Then [the ruled that] retaliation is not definite according to him, rather the guardian has a choice between retaliation and taking blood-money. Our [Hanafi] evidence: Wealth is only obligatory in [cases of] mistake necessarily to protect blood from being wasted, since there is no similitude between it [the weapon] and the soul; so in intentional [murder] it is not obligatory while the like is possible in form and meaning. (No expiation), contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and he says: It [expiation] is obligatory in mistake, so it is more appropriate that it be obligatory in intentional. And we say: The fact that expiation covers up mistake does not necessitate that it covers up intentional; and it [intentional] is a pure major sin. He said: 'Then retaliation is not definite'; meaning the obligatory is one of them, i.e. retaliation or blood-money, not specifically, and it becomes specific by the guardian's choice because the right of the servant is legislated as compensatory, and in each one is a type of compensation, so he chooses between retaliation and taking blood-money. He said: 'Our evidence: that wealth...' etc.; its clarification: wealth is not suitable as a cause due to lack of similitude, and qisas is suitable for similitude, and in it is the benefit of life, deterrence and compensation, so it becomes definite. In mistake: obligation of wealth is necessary to protect blood from being wasted, and it is not certain that the guardian will not [seek] intent after taking wealth, so it does not become definite to prevent destruction. He said: 'Then it is more appropriate that it be obligatory...' etc.; because the need for expiation in intentional is more pressing than it is in mistake, so intentional is more deserving of its obligation. And we [say]: Intentional is a pure major sin leading to the forbidden without any shade of permissibility, and in expiation is the meaning of worship, so it is not attached to its like; and because expiation is from the fixed measures of the law, and its specification in the law is to repel the lower, not specifically to repel the higher. And from its ruling is deprivation of inheritance even if not explicitly stated, but he indicated later by saying 'and no inheritance except here' as will come, and the proof of deprivation is his statement, peace be upon him: 'No inheritance for a killer.'
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory for intentional murder (qatl al-'amd). The Hanafi position, defended by the author, is that kaffarah is not required for intentional murder, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The proof begins by establishing that in intentional murder, the guardian (wali) has a choice between qisas (retaliation) and diyah (blood-money); qisas is not automatically determined. The Hanafi rationale: wealth (diyah) is only imposed in accidental killing (khatta) out of necessity to prevent the blood from being wasted, because there is no exact similitude between the weapon and the soul. In intentional murder, since a comparable act (qisas) is possible both in form and meaning, wealth is not imposed. Similarly, kaffarah is not imposed. Shafi'i argues: if kaffarah is obligatory in accidental killing, it should be even more so in intentional, since the need is greater. The Hanafi response: kaffarah covers up (sitr) accidental sin, but intentional murder is a pure major sin (kabirah mahdah) that has no semblance of permissibility. Kaffarah, being an act of worship, is not attached to such pure sin. Additionally, kaffarah is prescribed by the Lawgiver for lesser offenses, not for greater ones. The passage also notes that the killer is deprived of inheritance from the victim, citing the hadith 'The killer does not inherit.' This disciplinary point reinforces the gravity of intentional murder without adding kaffarah.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): A compensatory act of worship (like fasting or feeding the poor) prescribed to expiate sins; in jinayat, it is obligatory for accidental killing but not for intentional according to Hanafis.
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional killing with a weapon or instrument that usually kills, with intention to kill; it is a pure major sin.
الخطأ (al-khaṭa): Unintentional killing, e.g., by mistake or accident; kaffarah is wajib here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is kaffarah not obligatory for intentional murder (qatl al-'amd)?
A) Because intentional murder is a minor sin that does not require expiation.
B) Because intentional murder is a pure major sin with no shubhah, and kaffarah is for sins involving doubt.
C) Because the killer is already executed, so no expiation is needed.
D) Because kaffarah is only for accidental killings according to the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require kaffarah for intentional murder because it is a pure major sin lacking shubhah; the killer's penalty is qisas/diyah and inheritance deprivation, not expiation.

Shibh al-Amd: Definition and the Instrument Dispute
Defines shibh al-amd and resolves disagreement over instruments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of shibh al-amd and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the classification of large instruments.

Core Idea
Shibh al-amd: intentional strike, unintended death – instrument determines category.

This passage defines the category of quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-amd) and presents a key disagreement within the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifa considers striking with a large stone or stick as shibh al-amd, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it intentional murder. Understanding this dispute reveals how intent and instrument interact in Hanafi criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Define shibh al-amd: intentionally striking with an instrument that does not normally kill. Note that it incurs sin, expiation, and heavy diya on the 'aqila, but no qisas.
The passage also emphasizes that there is no shibh al-amd for injuries below the neck – those are fully intentional.
2
Locate the instrument dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that striking with a large stone or large stick is also shibh al-amd. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) say it is 'amd (intentional).
The Sahibayn also distinguish repeated blows with a small stick as 'amd.
3
Examine the proofs
For Abu Hanifa: the hadith 'the slain of the whip and the stick' and the argument that large instruments are not typically for killing. For the Sahibayn: qiyas – large instruments normally kill, so intent to strike implies intent to kill.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba from al-Hasan.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The text, quoting al-Hidayah, presents Abu Hanifa's evidence and appears to favor his view. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's.
However, in practice, many later Hanafis may follow the Sahibayn for caution.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the perpetrator pays heavy diya and no qisas. Under the Sahibayn, it is qisas (retaliation) if intentional murder is established.
This difference is critical in actual legal judgments.

وشبه العمد: ضربه قصداً بغير ما ذكر : كالعصا والسوط ، أو الحجر الصغير، وأما الضرب بالحجرِ العظيم، والخشب العظيم فمن شبه العمد أيضا عند أبي حنيفة له، خلافاً لغيره، (وفيه الإثم والكفارة، ودِيَةٌ مغلظة على العاقلة) ، سيأتي تفسيرُ الدِّيةِ المغلظة، وتفسير العاقلة إن شاء الله ، (بلا قود. وهو فيما دون النفس عمد) : أي ضربُهُ قصداً بغير ما ذكر فيما دونَ النَّفْسِ عمد موجب للقصاص ، فليس فيما دون النفس شبه عمد.

The Book of Offenses / Types of Killing and What Requires Retaliation and Testimony Thereon. And quasi-intentional (shibh al-amd): striking intentionally with other than what was mentioned (i.e., not with a weapon or something that normally kills), and in it there is sin and expiation, and a heavy blood-wit upon the 'aqila, without retaliation, and it is intentional regarding what is less than a life. And quasi-intentional: striking intentionally with other than what was mentioned: such as a stick or a whip, or a small stone. But striking with a large stone or a large piece of wood is also quasi-intentional according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to others. (And in it there is sin and expiation, and a heavy blood-wit upon the 'aqila). The interpretation of the heavy blood-wit and the 'aqila will come later, God willing. (Without retaliation. And it is intentional regarding what is less than a life): i.e., striking intentionally with other than what was mentioned regarding what is less than a life is intentional and requires retaliation, so there is no quasi-intentional regarding less than a life. [1] His saying: 'and quasi-intentional'; it is only called quasi-intentional because in the act there are two meanings: the meaning of intention with respect to the doer's intent to strike, and the meaning of error with respect to the absence of intent to kill given the instrument he used, for it is an instrument for striking for discipline, not for killing, and one only intends each act with its instrument, so that was an error resembling intention in form, in that he intended to strike and to commit what is forbidden to him. So in 'al-Kifayah'. [2] His saying: 'contrary to others'; he said in 'al-Hidayah': Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i: if he strikes him with a large stone or a large piece of wood, it is intentional, and quasi-intentional is that he intentionally strikes him with something that does not normally kill, like a small stick if he does not repeat the blows. If he repeats them, it is intentional, but the meaning of intentionality is limited by using a small instrument that does not normally kill, because he intends by it something other than killing, like discipline and the like, so it is quasi-intentional. And for Abu Hanifa is his saying, may God be pleased with him: 'Verily, the slain of the error of intention – i.e., quasi-intentional – the slain of the whip and the stick has one hundred camels' reported by Ibn Abi Shayba in his 'Musannaf' from al-Hasan, may God be pleased with him; and because the instrument is not placed for killing nor used for it.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage defines 'shibh al-amd' (quasi-intentional killing) and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the classification of certain instruments. The author first gives the basic ruling: shibh al-amd is striking intentionally with something that does not normally kill (like a whip, stick, or small stone). It incurs sin, expiation, and a heavy blood-wit on the 'aqila (the offender's male paternal relatives), but not retaliation (qisas). Notably, there is no shibh al-amd for wounds less than life; such acts are fully intentional and require qisas. The dispute centers on striking with a large stone or large stick: Abu Hanifa considers it shibh al-amd, while Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Imam al-Shafi'i consider it intentional (qatl 'amd). Abu Hanifa supports his view with a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that categorizes killing with a whip or stick as quasi-intentional, and he argues that such large instruments are not typically used for killing. The Sahibayn differentiate based on whether the instrument normally kills; if repeated blows are used, it becomes intentional. The commentary emphasizes that shibh al-amd combines elements of intent (to hit) and error (not intending death due to the instrument's nature).

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-amd): A category of killing where the offender intentionally strikes but does not intend to kill, using an instrument that does not normally cause death.
دية مغلظة (diyat mughallazah): A heavy blood-wit, which for shibh al-amd is one hundred camels (or equivalent), paid by the 'aqila over three years.
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The male paternal relatives (asabah) of the offender who are liable to pay the diyah in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Quasi-intentional killing (shibh al-amd)
C) Accidental killing (khata)
D) It is not considered killing

Key Takeaway
Shibh al-amd is a distinct category in Hanafi law where the instrument, not just the intent, determines the ruling, and the intra-school disagreement on large instruments hinges on the perceived normal use of the tool.

Khata: Two Types of Accidental Killing
Explains accidental killing and its two types: error in intent and error in action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between error in intent and error in action in accidental killing, and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Accidental killing has two types – error in intent and error in action – both require diyah but no qisas.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi taxonomy of accidental killing (khata). It divides it into two types based on whether the error lies in the intention or in the physical act. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of diyah and expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its categorization
The passage discusses accidental killing (khata) and clarifies that it is not a single category but two: error in intention and error in action. Recognize that the act is involuntary in terms of the intended victim.
The key is that the killer did not intend to kill the specific victim.
2
Distinguish the two types
Error in intent (khata fi al-qasd) occurs when the doer intends a lawful act but misidentifies the target, e.g., shooting a Muslim thinking he is a deer. Error in action (khata fi al-fi'l) occurs when the intention and target are correct but the action fails, e.g., shooting at a target but hitting a person.
Use the examples given: 'رمى مسلماً ظنه صيداً' for error in intent, and 'رمى غرضاً فأصاب آدمياً' for error in action.
3
Understand the basis for the distinction
The commentator explains that a person acts with both the heart (intention) and the limbs (action). Error can affect either independently or in combination. This dual nature is why khata is split into two types.
This is a conceptual foundation: the human act has two loci – intention and physical movement.
4
Note the implications for sin and liability
The hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' removes the sin of the killing itself (no sin for intending to kill, as there was no intent). However, the commentator notes that there is a minor sin for negligence (ترك العزيمة). Diyah is wajib on the 'aqila, and kaffarah may also apply.
The ruling: no qisas, diyah wajib, no major sin, but liability for lack of caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

،
[١]قوله : الخطأ ضربان ; وإنّما صار الخطأ نوعين; لأنّ الإنسان يتصرف بفعل القلب والجوارح ، فيحملُ الخطأ في كلّ واحدٍ منهما على الانفراد، كمـا ذكـره الشارح له أو على الاجتماع، بأن رمى آدمياً ظنّه صيداً فأصاب غيره من الناس. كذا في الزيلعي (1) [۲] قوله : كما إذا رمى الغَرَض ; وهو - بالغين المعجمة، والراء المهملة المفتوحتين، وآخره ضاد معجمة - : الهدف الذي يرمي فيه، والرامي يظنّه ذلك، وهو في نفس الأمر كذلك، فلا خطأ في هذا القصد، وإنّما الخطأ في فعله الذي هو إيصالُ السهم إلى غير ما قصده.
[٣] قوله : وليس في الخطأ ... الخ ; أي في النوعين منه إثم القتل ; أي إثم قصدِ القتل ; لقوله : «رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان».
وأما القتل في نفسه فلا يعرى عن الإثم من حيث ترك العزيمة كما ذكره الشارح .
And regarding error (khata), even if against a slave intentionally: like shooting a Muslim whom he thought was game or a combatant, or an action: like shooting at a target and hitting a human. [1] His saying: 'error is two types'; it only became two types because the human acts with the action of the heart and the limbs, so error occurs in each of them separately, as the commentator mentioned, or in combination, such as shooting a human thinking he was game and hitting another person. Thus in al-Zayla'i. [2] His saying: 'as when he shoots at a target'; it (al-gharad) is - with the letter ghayn and ra', both open, and ending with dad - the target at which one shoots, and the shooter thinks it is that, and in reality it is so, so there is no error in this intent; the error is only in his action, which is delivering the arrow to other than what he intended. [3] His saying: 'and in error there is not... etc.'; i.e., in both types there is no sin of killing, i.e., sin of intending to kill, because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'My community is forgiven for error and forgetfulness.' As for the killing itself, it is not free from sin in terms of abandoning caution, as the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage defines accidental killing (khata) and divides it into two types: error in intent (khata fi al-qasd) and error in action (khata fi al-fi'l). Error in intent occurs when the doer intends a permissible act but mistakenly targets the wrong person or object – e.g., shooting at a Muslim thinking he is a game animal or an enemy combatant. Error in action occurs when the doer intends a lawful act (like shooting at a target) but misses and hits a person. The commentary explains that the distinction arises because a person acts with both inner intention and physical action; error can affect either. The text also notes that in both types, there is no sin for the intention to kill (since it was absent), but there is a sin for lack of precaution, as the hadith 'My community is forgiven for error and forgetfulness' indicates. However, the killing itself still carries some sin for not exercising caution, as the commentator points out. The passage also mentions that accidental killing of a slave also incurs diyah on the 'aqila, correcting a possible misconception that since a slave is property, his loss would be only monetary.

Key Terms
الخطأ (al-khata): Accidental killing where there is no intent to harm; divided into error in intent and error in action.
خطأ في القصد (khata fi al-qasd): Error in the intention: the doer intends a lawful act but misidentifies the target (e.g., shooting a Muslim thinking he is a deer).
خطأ في الفعل (khata fi al-fi'l): Error in the action: the doer intends a lawful act and correct target but misses and hits another (e.g., shooting at a target and hitting a person).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Error in intention (khata fi al-qasd)
B) Error in action (khata fi al-fi'l)
C) Intentional killing ('amd)
D) Quasi-intentional (shibh al-amd)

Key Takeaway
Accidental killing in Hanafi fiqh is either error in intent or error in action, both requiring diyah and expiation, but no qisas or sin for the killing act itself.

Ruling on Indirect Killing: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on indirect killing consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct and indirect killing in Hanafi law and state the Hanafi ruling on kaffarah and inheritance deprivation in cases of indirect killing.

Core Idea
Indirect killing incurs diya but no kaffarah or inheritance deprivation – the sin is in the killing, not the cause.

This passage addresses a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the legal consequences of indirectly causing someone's death. While both schools agree that blood-money (diya) is payable by the 'a'qila, they diverge on whether expiation (kaffarah) and deprivation of inheritance apply. The Hanafi position, grounded in the concept that the killing is not actual, restricts these obligations to direct accidental killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the distinction between (a) a sleeping person falling on another (direct causation) and (b) indirect killing via placing a stone or digging a well. The former incurs kaffarah and diya; the latter only diya.
Direct causation is treated as quasi-accidental; indirect causation is a lesser category.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
In indirect killing, the killer pays diya through the 'a'qila but owes no kaffarah and is not deprived of inheritance from the victim.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Recognize the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes indirect killing to accidental killing (khata') in all respects, thus requiring kaffarah and inheritance deprivation.
The analogy is based on the outcome – death caused by a prohibited act.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The killing is not actual (ma'dum haqiqatan); the analogy applies only to worldly compensation (daman), not to religious obligations (kaffarah) or inheritance, which attach to the actual sin of killing.
The sin of the digger is the sin of digging, not of killing.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a person who causes death indirectly (e.g., setting a trap) does not forfeit inheritance and does not perform kaffarah in Hanafi law, unlike in Shafi'i law.
This difference affects estate planning and expiatory duties.

وما جرى مجراه : كنائم سقط على آخر فقتله كفارةٌ ودِيَةٌ على عاقلته. وفي القتل بسبب كتلفه بوضع حجر ، وحفر بئر في غير ملكه دِيَةٌ على العاقلة بلا كفارة ولا إرث إلا هنا ( ) ، هذا عندنا ، وعندَ الشَّافعي) تجب الكفارة، ويثبت به حرمان الميراث إلحاقاً بالخطأ، قلنا : القتل معدوم حقيقة، والحق بالخطأ في حق الضمان، ففى غيره بقى على أصله.
قال في «الكفاية»(۲) : وهذا الإثم إثم القتل ؛ لأنَّ نفس تركِ المبالغة في الاحتياط ليس باثم، وإنّما يصير به آثماً إذا اتصل به القتل فتقصر الكفارة لذنب القتل، وإن لم يكن فيه إثم قصد القتل. هكذا في الجلبي له

قلنا : هو وإن كان يأثم بالحفر في غير ملكه إلا أنَّ حرمان الإرثِ إِنَّما يتعلّق على الإثم الحاصل بنفس القتل، وما ذكرتم ليس كذلك، فإن إثمه إثم الحفر لا الموت).
The Book of Homicides / Types of killing and what necessitates retaliation (qisas) and testimony upon it. And what runs in its course: like a sleeping person who falls upon another and kills him – there is expiation (kaffarah) and blood-money (diya) upon his 'a'qila (family). And in killing by causation like destroying by placing a stone, and digging a well in other than his property – blood-money upon the 'a'qila without expiation nor inheritance except here. This is in our view, and according to al-Shafi'i, expiation is obligatory, and deprivation of inheritance is established by analogy to accidental killing (khata'). We said: Killing is non-existent in reality, and it is analogized to accidental killing in respect of compensation, so in other than that it remains upon its original principle. It is said in al-Kifaya: This sin is the sin of killing; because the mere omission of being extremely cautious is not a sin, but one becomes sinful by it only when killing is connected to it, so the expiation is confined to the sin of killing, even if there is no sin of intention of killing. Thus in al-Jalabi. If it is said: The digger in other than his property sins, and the sin of killing is present, so deprivation of inheritance can be attached to it, as you mentioned in accidental killing. We said: Although he sins by digging in other than his property, the deprivation of inheritance is attached only to the sin resulting from the killing itself, and what you mentioned is not like that, for his sin is the sin of digging, not of death.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the legal consequences of indirect killing (qatl bi-sabab). The author first states the Hanafi ruling: if someone causes death by indirect means, such as placing a stone or digging a well on non-owned land, the blood-money (diya) is payable by the 'a'qila (the paternal relatives), but there is no expiation (kaffarah) and the killer does not forfeit inheritance from the deceased. However, a sleeping person who falls on and kills another incurs both expiation and blood-money, analogized to accidental killing (khata'). The Shafi'i school analogizes all indirect killing to accidental killing, thus requiring expiation and inheritance deprivation. The Hanafi response argues that killing is not actual (ma'dum haqiqatan) in indirect causation; it is considered only for compensation (daman) but not for religious obligations (kaffarah) or inheritance consequences. The text then cites al-Kifaya to clarify that the sin involved is the sin of killing itself, not the act of negligence. Finally, an objection is raised: the digger sins by unlawfully digging, so why not attach inheritance deprivation? The answer: the sin of digging is distinct from the sin of causing death; inheritance deprivation is tied specifically to the sin of killing, not to a prior unlawful act. Thus, the Hanafi position maintains a strict distinction between direct and indirect causation in matters of expiation and inheritance.

Key Terms
قتل بسبب (qatl bi-sabab): Killing through an indirect cause, such as placing a stone or digging a well, where the act is not directly lethal but leads to death.
عاقلة ('a'qila): The paternal relatives of the killer who are liable to pay diya in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.
حرمان الإرث (hurman al-irth): Deprivation of inheritance for the killer; a person who causes the death of the deceased may be barred from inheriting.
كفارة (kaffarah): An expiatory act required in accidental killing, such as freeing a slave or fasting.
خطأ (khata'): Accidental killing without intent, where the act itself is not unlawful but results in death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on kaffarah (expiation) for a person who kills another by placing a stone in a non-owned pathway?
A) Kaffarah is obligatory because it is analogous to accidental killing.
B) Kaffarah is not obligatory because killing is not actual in indirect causation.
C) Kaffarah is obligatory only if the stone was placed with intent to kill.
D) Kaffarah is replaced by an additional diya.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, indirect killing (qatl bi-sabab) results in diya on the 'a'qila but does not require kaffarah or bar inheritance, unlike direct accidental killing.

Qisas for Free Killing Slave: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
General rule for qisas and dispute over free killing slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on executing a free person for killing a slave and explain the difference with the Shafi'i school using the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
A free person is executed for killing a slave because the slave's blood is perpetually protected.

This passage introduces the general condition for qisas: intentional killing of a person whose blood is perpetually inviolable. It then presents a key intra-Islamic dispute: while the Hanafi school holds that a free person is executed for killing a slave, Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, citing the Quranic verse 'The free for the free, and the slave for the slave.' Understanding how each school interprets this verse is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to textual evidence and the role of overarching principles.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for qisas
Identify the two conditions mentioned: the killing must be intentional, and the victim's blood must be perpetually protected. 'Perpetually' excludes temporary protection (e.g., musta'min).
This rule is the foundation for the dispute.
2
Identify the contested case
The dispute is whether a free person who intentionally kills a slave is subject to qisas. State the Hanafi position (yes) and the Shafi'i position (no).
The disagreement arises from the interpretation of a single Quranic verse.
3
Analyze each school's proof
Examine the verse (2:178) cited by al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi response is that the verse establishes equivalence in retaliation, not a restriction. Both schools use the same text but derive opposite rulings.
Note that the verse itself does not explicitly forbid executing a free person for a slave.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the counterargument
The Hanafi school argues that the slave's blood is protected, and the general rule of qisas applies. The verse merely illustrates the principle of reciprocity, not a complete list of who can be killed for whom.
This is a classic example of interpreting a general text (amm) as not excluding specific cases.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, a free killer of a slave faces execution; under Shafi'i law, he only pays diya (compensation). This difference affects actual legal outcomes.
The mufta bihi within the Hanafi school is the view that a free person is killed for a slave.

Chapter on what necessitates retribution and what does not. It is obligatory by killing someone whose blood is perpetually protected, intentionally. So the free person is killed for the free person and for the slave. This is our [Hanafi] position, and according to al-Shafi'i, the free person is not killed for the slave; because of His saying: 'The free for the free, and the slave for the slave.'
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the conditions that necessitate qisas (retributive execution). The general rule is that qisas is obligatory when a person intentionally kills someone whose blood is perpetually inviolable. 'Perpetually' excludes a musta'min (a non-Muslim given temporary safe passage) whose blood is protected only temporarily. The Hanafi school holds that a free person is executed for killing a slave, while Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, citing the Quranic verse (2:178) which sets a principle of matching types: free for free, slave for slave. The Hanafi argument, explained in the commentary, is that this verse establishes equivalence in value, not a bar against killing a free person for a slave; rather, the inequality in social status does not negate the sanctity of life. The full discussion includes further reasoning and responses to objections.

Key Terms
القود (al-qawad): Retributive execution (qisas) for intentional homicide.
حُقِنَ دمُهُ (huqina damuhu): His blood is protected (inviolable), meaning it is unlawful to kill him.
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A non-Muslim who is given safe passage (aman) into Muslim lands temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a free person is executed for killing a slave because the slave's blood is perpetually protected, interpreting the Quranic verse as a principle of equivalence rather than a restriction.

Qisas Between Free and Slave: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Text establishes Hanafi qisas ruling and refutes Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on qisas between a free person and a slave, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the interpretation of Qur'anic verses.

Core Idea
General verses on qisas override the specific mention in 2:178 due to contextual interpretation (al-ahd).

In this lesson, we examine a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether a free person can be executed in qisas for killing a slave. The disagreement centers on the interpretation of Qur'anic verses and the principle of equality in inviolability. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze textual evidence and the role of context in usul.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi principle for qisas
Identify the two conditions: intentional killing and victim's blood protected forever.
This sets the foundation for the dispute.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its textual basis
Quote the verse 2:178 and the Shafi'i interpretation that it restricts qisas to equals.
The key verse: 'The free for the free, the slave for the slave.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Explain that the definite article 'al' is for a known group (al-ahd), not the genus, based on the historical context of Banu al-Nadir and Banu Qurayza.
This allows the general verses (5:45, 2:178) to remain operative.
4
Examine the rationale: equality in isma
Both free and slave share inviolability (isma) by virtue of being in Dar al-Islam or having dhimmah.
Equality in freedom is not required; only equality in inviolability.
5
Conclude with the practical difference
Thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi allows qisas; Shafi'i only diyah.
This highlights the real-world impact of the disagreement.

Chapter on what necessitates qisas and what does not. It is obligatory by killing one whose blood is protected forever intentionally, so a free person is killed for a free person and for a slave. (Commentary: 'forever' excludes the musta'min, whose blood protection is temporary until his return. This is our position and according to al-Shafi'i: a free person is not killed for a slave, due to His saying: 'The free for the free, the slave for the slave.') Then the Hanafi proof: 'And We prescribed for them therein that the soul for the soul' (5:45), and 'Prescribed for you is retaliation in murder' (2:178), and 'Intentional killing is qisas.' The restricted mention does not oppose the general. The verse was revealed about Banu al-Nadir and Banu Qurayza, specifying the 'lam' for known individuals, not for the genus. They are equal in inviolability, which is by religion according to him and by abode according to us, so qisas applies between them.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. The author begins by stating the general principle that qisas (retaliation) is wajib for intentionally killing someone whose blood is inviolable (ma haqina damuhu abadan). 'Forever' excludes the musta'min (a non-Muslim given temporary safe conduct), as his inviolability is temporary. Then he presents the Hanafi ruling: a free person may be killed in qisas for killing a slave, and also for killing a free person. This is followed by the Shafi'i objection: based on the Qur'anic verse (2:178) 'The free for the free, the slave for the slave', the Shafi'i school holds that retaliation must be limited to equals—free for free, slave for slave—so a free person cannot be killed for a slave. The Hanafi response is that this verse was revealed in a specific historical context (the practice of the tribes Banu al-Nadir and Banu Qurayza where they exchanged individuals of different status) and the definite article 'al' here indicates a known group (al-ahd), not the genus. Thus it does not restrict the general verses like 'the soul for the soul' (5:45) and the command for qisas (2:178). Furthermore, the Hanafi argue that what matters for qisas is equality in inviolability (isma), which is based on religion (for Shafi'i) or abode (dar, for Hanafi), not on freedom or slavery. If equality in other aspects were required, qisas would not apply between male and female, which is agreed upon. The free person retains humanity (adamiyya) and is not property in this context; hence a slave is killed for a slave, and a free person is killed for a slave. The inviolability overrides other considerations like infirmity or apostasy remnants.

Key Terms
القود (al-qawad): Retaliatory punishment (qisas) for intentional killing or injury.
حَقِنَ دَمُهُ (haqina damuhu): One whose blood is inviolable and protected, i.e., a Muslim or a dhimmi.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim territory with temporary safe conduct (aman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The opinion of the Sahaba
D) Analogy to property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows qisas between free and slave based on general Qur'anic texts and equality in inviolability, while the Shafi'i school restricts it using the specific verse.
