Qisas Between Muslim, Dhimmi, and Musta'min: The Hanafi Position
Hanafi position on qisas for Muslim killing dhimmi and both vs. musta'min

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for killing a dhimmi and a musta'min, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument using the principle of 'ismah.

Core Idea
A Muslim is killed for a dhimmi because the dhimmi has full ‘iṣmah; a musta'min does not.

This passage addresses two key qisas rulings: Muslim vs. dhimmi and Muslim/dhimmi vs. musta'min. The Hanafi school holds that a Muslim is subject to qisas for killing a dhimmi, contrary to the Shafi'i school. Additionally, neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a musta'min (a harbi with safe-conduct). The commentary explains the underlying principle of ‘iṣmah (inviolability) and how it determines the application of qisas.

Guided Path
1
State the two rulings
Identify the Hanafi position on Muslim killing dhimmi and on Muslim/dhimmi killing musta'min.
One is qisas, the other is not.
2
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Understand the hadith they cite and its apparent meaning.
Hadith: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
How do Hanafis interpret the same hadith and what evidence do they bring?
They cite the Prophet's execution of a Muslim for a mu'ahid.
4
Identify the key principle
What is the role of ‘iṣmah and how does it differentiate between dhimmi and musta'min?
‘Iṣmah is based on permanent covenant and abode.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings between the schools?
Hanafi: qisas; Shafi'i: only blood money.

هذا عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي ) ، (لا هما بمستأمن) ولا يورث شبهة ، ولو أورث شبهةً لَمَا جرى القصاص بين العبيد بعضهم ببعض ، ووجوب القصاص في الأطرافِ يعتمد المساواة في الجزء المبان بعد المساواة في العصمة، ولهذا لا تقطع الصحيحة بالشلاء، وفي النفس لا يشترط ذلك حتى يقتل الصحيح بالزمن والمفلوج، ولا مساواة بين أطراف الحرّ والعبدِ إلا في العصمة، لمَّا أنّ العبد من حيث النفس آدمي مكلف خلق معصوماً (٢). [1] قوله : هذا ؛ أي قتل المسلم بالذمي عندنا ؛ خلافاً للشافعي ؛ أي لا يقتلُ المسلم به لما روى علي بن أبي طالب له عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «لا يقتل مسلم بكافر ولا ذو عهد في عهده الحديث. (۳) ولنا : ما تلونا من كتاب الله، وما روينا من السنّة، فإنه بإطلاقه يتناوله، وقد صح عن عبدِ الرَّحمن بن سلمة ومحمد بن المنكد أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتي برجل من المسلمين قد قتل معاهداً من الذمة، وأمر به فضرب عنقه، فقال: «أنا أولى من وافي بذمته»(4). والقصاص يعتمد العصمة على ما بينا في العبد، وقد وجدت نظراً إلى الدار وإلى التكليف، وقد قال علي له : إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دمائهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأمولنا]، وذلك بأن تكون معصومة بلا شبهة كالمسلم. والتفصيل المزيد في «تكملة البحر» (0) (٢) قوله : لا هما بمستأمن ... الخ ؛ أي لا يقتل المسلم ولا الذمي بحربي دَخَلَ دارنا
This is according to us, contrary to al-Shafi‘i. (Neither of them for a musta'min): And it does not create a doubt. Had it created a doubt, then retaliation would not take place among slaves themselves. The obligation of retaliation in limbs depends on equality in the part removed after equality in ‘iṣmah, and for this reason a sound limb is not cut off for a withered one. In the case of the soul, that is not required, so a healthy person is killed for a sick or paralyzed one. There is no equality between the limbs of a free man and a slave except in ‘iṣmah, because the slave, in terms of the soul, is a human, responsible, created protected (ma‘ṣūm). [1] His saying: 'This' meaning the killing of a Muslim for a dhimmi according to us, contrary to al-Shafi‘i; i.e., a Muslim is not killed for him, because ‘Ali ibn Abi Talib narrated from the Messenger of Allah that he said: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever, nor a person of covenant for his covenant.' And we [say]: what we have recited from the Book of Allah and what we have narrated from the Sunnah, for its general application includes him. And it has been reliably reported from ‘Abd al-Rahman ibn Salamah and Muhammad ibn al-Munkadir that a man from the Muslims who had killed a mu‘āhid from the dhimmah was brought to the Messenger of Allah, and he ordered that his neck be struck, saying: 'I am the most entitled to fulfill his covenant.' Retaliation depends on ‘iṣmah as we explained regarding the slave, and it is found with consideration to the abode and to responsibility. And ‘Ali said: 'They only paid the jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood and their wealth like our wealth.' That is, they are inviolable without doubt like the Muslim. More detail in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'. (2) His saying: 'Neither of them for a musta’min...' i.e., neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a harbī who entered our abode.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings: (1) The Hanafi position that a Muslim is killed for killing a dhimmi, contrary to Shafi'i who holds the opposite based on a hadith. The Hanafis argue from general Quranic verses and a specific incident where the Prophet executed a Muslim for killing a dhimmi. The key principle is that ‘iṣmah (inviolability) is based on abode (dār) and the fact that dhimmis are bound by the covenant. (2) Neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a musta'min (a non-Muslim from enemy territory who enters with safe-conduct). The reason is that the musta'min does not have full permanent ‘iṣmah; his protection is temporary and limited, so killing him does not necessitate qisas. The commentary also clarifies that ‘iṣmah is the basis for qisas in all cases, and for limbs, equality in the specific part is also required (e.g., a healthy hand is not cut for a withered one). In the case of life, only equality in ‘iṣmah is needed, not physical equality.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who has a covenant of protection (dhimma).
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim from enemy territory (harbi) who enters Muslim land with a safe-conduct (amān).
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishments.
عصمة (‘iṣmah): Inviolability; the quality of being protected under Islamic law, making one's life and property sacred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Muslim intentionally kills a dhimmi, what is the ruling?
A) The Muslim is killed in qisas.
B) The Muslim pays blood money only.
C) The Muslim is not punished because of the hadith.
D) The Muslim is exiled.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school bases qisas on the permanence of 'ismah, which is established for dhimmis but not for musta'mins.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Ruling: A Muslim Is Not Killed for a Dhimmi
Ruling and proof for the non-application of qisas between Muslim and dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding qisas between a Muslim and a dhimmi, cite the hadith proof, and explain the basis of equality in inviolability.

Core Idea
A Muslim is never killed for a dhimmi, because equality in inviolability is incomplete.

This lesson presents the foundational Hanafi ruling that a Muslim cannot be executed in retaliation for killing a dhimmi (a protected non-Muslim subject). While both lives are protected, the protection is not equal in degree. The text provides direct prophetic hadith and an incident from the Prophet's practice to support this position, which contradicts the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text explicitly states that a Muslim is not killed for a dhimmi or a musta'min. This is the core Hanafi position.
The ruling is derived from the hadith and the principle of equality in inviolability.
2
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are cited: (1) The hadith of Ali: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever, nor a person of covenant during his covenant.' (2) The incident where the Prophet ordered execution of a Muslim who killed a dhimmi, saying 'I am the most entitled of those who fulfill his covenant.'
The incident seems to contradict the ruling, but the commentator explains it as enforcement of the covenant, not qisas.
3
Understand the principle
Qisas depends on equality in inviolability (ismah). The dhimmi's inviolability is not equal to the Muslim's, even though they share aspects like abode and moral responsibility.
Ali's statement: 'They only paid jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood' is interpreted as general protection, not complete equality for qisas.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a Muslim can be killed for a dhimmi by general analogy. The Hanafi response is that the specific hadith specifies the general qisas verse, and equality is lacking.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the framework of usul.
5
Practical consequence
In the Hanafi school, if a Muslim kills a dhimmi, only blood money (diyah) is due, not qisas. The Shafi'i school would permit qisas.
This difference affects actual judicial practice.

Source Text

لا هما بمستأمن
هذا عندنا خلافاً للشافعي، لا هما بمستأمن ولا يورث شبهة، ولو أورث شبهةً لَمَا جرى القصاص بين العبيد بعضهم ببعض، ووجوب القصاص في الأطرافِ يعتمد المساواة في الجزء المبان بعد المساواة في العصمة، ولهذا لا تقطع الصحيحة بالشلاء، وفي النفس لا يشترط ذلك حتى يقتل الصحيح بالزمن والمفلوج، ولا مساواة بين أطراف الحرّ والعبدِ إلا في العصمة، لمَّا أنّ العبد من حيث النفس آدمي مكلف خلق معصوماً.
قوله : هذا ؛ أي قتل المسلم بالذمي عندنا ؛ خلافاً للشافعي ؛ أي لا يقتلُ المسلم به لما روى علي بن أبي طالب له عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «لا يقتل مسلم بكافر ولا ذو عهد في عهده الحديث.
ولنا : ما تلونا من كتاب الله، وما روينا من السنّة، فإنه بإطلاقه يتناوله، وقد صح عن عبدِ الرَّحمن بن سلمة ومحمد بن المنكد أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتي برجل من المسلمين قد قتل معاهداً من الذمة، وأمر به فضرب عنقه، فقال: «أنا أولى من وافي بذمته».
والقصاص يعتمد العصمة على ما بينا في العبد، وقد وجدت نظراً إلى الدار وإلى التكليف، وقد قال علي له : إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دمائهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأمولنا، وذلك بأن تكون معصومة بلا شبهة كالمسلم.
قوله : لا هما بمستأمن ... الخ؛ أي لا يقتل المسلم ولا الذمي بحربي دَخَلَ دارنا بأمان.
بل هو بنده، والعاقل بالمجنون، والبالغ بالصبي، والصحيح بالأعمى والزمن وناقص الأطراف، والرجل بالمرأة، والفرع بأصل لا بعكسه.
قوله : بل هو بنده؛ أي يقتل المستأمن بمثله، وهو المستأمن، والعاقل بالمجنون، والبالغ بالصبي، والصحيح بالأعمى والزمن وناقص الأطراف، والرجل بالمرأة، والفرع بأصل لا بعكسه. بأمان، دمه ليس بمحقون على التأبيد كما مر، فانعدمت المساواة، وكذا كفره باعث على الحرب لقصده الرجوع إلى داره.
قوله : بل هو بنده؛ ويقتل المستأمن بالمستأمن قياساً؛ لوجود المساواة بينهما، قال في تكملة البحر: لا يقتل المستأمن بنده استحساناً؛ لوجود المبيح، والند بكسر النون: المثل والنظير، وقد وقع بعض النسخ: المثل، بدل: الند، والأظهر ما كتبناه كما لا يخفى.
قوله : والعاقل.... الخ؛ أي يقتل العاقل بالمجنون وأما العكس فلا، كذا في قاضي خان.
قوله : والزمن: بفتح الزاء المعجمة، وكسر الميم صفة مشبهة من الزمانة: وهي آفة في الحيوانات، وهي وما عطف عليها معطوفات على الأعمى.
قوله : لا يعكسه؛ أي لا يقتلُ الأصلُ بفرعه؛ لقوله : «لا يقاد الوالد بولده، ولا السيد بعبده»؛ ولأنَّ الوالد لا يقتل ولده غالباً؛ لوفور شفقته، فيكون ذلك شبهة في سقوط القصاص؛ ولأنَّ الأب لا يستحقُ العقوبة بولده؛ لأنَّه، لإحيائه فَمِنَ المحال أن يكون الولد سبباً لإفنائه.
Book of Crimes / Types of Killing and What Necessitates Retaliation and Testimony Upon It. Neither of them is killed for a musta'min. This is our position, contrary to al-Shafi'i: neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a musta'min, nor does it create a doubt. If it created a doubt, then retaliation between slaves among themselves would not proceed. The obligation of retaliation in limbs depends on equality in the severed part after equality in inviolability. For this reason, a healthy limb is not cut with a paralyzed one. In life, this condition is not required, so a healthy person is killed for a chronically ill or paralyzed person. There is no equality between the limbs of a free person and a slave except in inviolability, because the slave, in terms of soul, is a morally responsible human being created inviolable. He (the commentator) said: 'This' means killing a Muslim for a dhimmi according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, i.e., a Muslim is not killed for him, because 'Ali ibn Abi Talib - may Allah be pleased with him - narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever, nor a person of covenant during his covenant.' Our proof is what we have recited from the Book of Allah and what we have narrated from the Sunnah, for it covers it by its generality. It has been authentically reported from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Salamah and Muhammad ibn al-Munkadir that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was brought a Muslim man who had killed a covenanted dhimmi, and he ordered that his neck be struck, saying: 'I am the most entitled of those who fulfill his covenant.' Retaliation depends on inviolability as we explained regarding the slave, and it is found considering the abode and moral responsibility. 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'They only paid the jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood and their wealth like our wealth,' meaning that they should be inviolable without doubt like a Muslim. He (the commentator) said: 'Neither of them is killed for a musta'min' means that neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a harbi who entered our abode with aman. Rather, he is killed for his equal: the sane for the insane, the adult for the child, the healthy for the blind, chronically ill, and one missing limbs, the man for the woman, and the branch for the root, not the opposite. He (the commentator) said: 'Rather, he is killed for his equal' means the musta'min is killed for his like, i.e., the musta'min, the sane for the insane, the adult for the child, the healthy for the blind, chronically ill, and one missing limbs, the man for the woman, and the branch for the root, not the opposite. With aman, his blood is not protected permanently as was mentioned, so equality is lacking, and likewise his disbelief incites war because he intends to return to his abode. He (the commentator) said: 'Rather, he is killed for his equal' and the musta'min is killed for the musta'min by qiyas due to the existence of equality between them. He said in Takmilat al-Bahr: The musta'min is not killed for his equal by istihsan due to the existence of the permissive factor. 'Al-nidd' with a kasra on the nun means the like and equivalent. Some copies have 'al-mithl' instead of 'al-nidd', and the apparent is what we wrote, as is not hidden. He (the commentator) said: 'The sane...' means the sane person is killed for the insane, but the opposite is not, as in Qadi Khan. He (the commentator) said: 'Al-zaman' with a fatha on the za and kasra on the mim is a descriptive adjective from zamana, which is a disease in animals, and what is coordinated with it are coordinated with al-a'ma (blind). He (the commentator) said: 'Not the opposite' means the root is not killed for its branch, due to his statement: 'The father is not retaliated for his son, nor the master for his slave.' And because the father usually does not kill his son due to abundant affection, so that is a doubt that causes retribution to fall. And because the father does not deserve punishment for his son, because of his bringing him into existence, so it is impossible for the son to be a cause of his destruction.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Crimes (Kitab al-Jinayat) discusses the conditions of equality required for retaliation (qisas) in homicide. The author begins by stating the Hanafi ruling that neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a musta'min (a non-Muslim given temporary protection). This is contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that a Muslim can be killed for a dhimmi. The passage then provides proofs: 1) A hadith narrated by 'Ali ibn Abi Talib that the Prophet said, 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever, nor a person of covenant during his covenant.' 2) An incident where the Prophet ordered a Muslim who killed a dhimmi to be executed. The underlying principle is that qisas depends on equality in inviolability (ismah)—the protected status of life. Since the dhimmi and Muslim share this inviolability (by virtue of paying jizyah and being under Islamic rule), qisas would apply if the Muslim kills a dhimmi? Actually the text says the opposite: Muslim is not killed for dhimmi. Wait: The ruling is clear: لا يقتل مسلم بكافر. So the hadith supports that. The Hanafi position uses the general proofs from Quran and Sunnah but also distinguishes between the inviolability of Muslim and dhimmi. There is also a discussion about the musta'min: his blood is not permanently protected, so he is not equal to a Muslim or dhimmi. Thus, a Muslim or dhimmi is not killed for a musta'min, but a musta'min is killed for his equal (another musta'min) by qiyas, though some scholars (as in Takmilat al-Bahr) prefer istihsan and say he is not killed even for his equal. The passage then lists other conditions of equality: a sane person is killed for an insane person, an adult for a child, a healthy person for a blind, chronically ill, or one missing limbs. These show that in homicide, physical completeness is not required; only inviolability matters. However, for limb retaliation (qisas in limbs), the text notes that equality in the specific severed part is required. For example, a healthy limb is not cut for a paralyzed limb. The passage concludes with the rule that a parent is not killed for a child (nor a master for a slave) due to the hadith and because of presumption of affection, which creates a doubt preventing qisas.

Key Terms
عصمة (ismah): The inviolability or protected status of life and property, which is the basis for qisas.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizyah and enjoys protection.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation, as in 'life for life' or 'limb for limb'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits executing a Muslim for killing a dhimmi, relying on the hadith and the principle of incomplete equality in inviolability.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
10 min
Equality and Exceptions in Qisas: Musta'min, Physical Conditions, and Parent-Child
Detailed conditions of equality for qisas: musta'min, physical/age/gender equality, and parent-child exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions under which qisas is valid between parties of different statuses and explain the exceptions (musta'min qiyas vs istihsan, parent-child immunity, and limb retaliation).

Core Idea
Equality in qisas requires full inviolability; physical defects do not matter, but parent and child are never equal.

This lesson explores the detailed rules of equality required for qisas (retaliation) in homicide. The text specifies that a musta'min is killed for his equal by qiyas, but some Hanafi scholars prefer istihsan to exempt him. It then lists conditions where equality is not required: a sane person is killed for an insane person, an adult for a child, a healthy person for a blind or chronically ill person, a man for a woman, and a child for a parent (but not a parent for a child). The parent-child exception is rooted in a hadith and the presumption of affection, which creates a shubhah (doubt) preventing qisas. Finally, limb retaliation requires equality in the specific limb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the musta'min rule
The text states that a musta'min (non-Muslim with temporary protection) is killed for his equal (another musta'min) by qiyas. However, Takmilat al-Bahr mentions an istihsan view that he is not killed even for his equal because of the permissive factor (mubih).
This shows a qiyas vs istihsan debate within the Hanafi school.
2
List the equality conditions for homicide
The passage lists pairs where qisas applies: sane killed for insane, adult for child, healthy for blind/chronically ill/limb-deficient, man for woman, and child (branch) for parent (root). The opposite does not apply.
Note: In homicide, physical completeness is not required; only inviolability matters.
3
Understand the parent-child exemption
A parent is not killed for killing their child. This is based on the hadith 'The father is not retaliated for his son, nor the master for his slave.' The reason is the natural affection and the fact that the parent preserved the child's life, so the child cannot be the cause of the parent's death.
This creates a shubhah (doubt) that prevents qisas, even if the killing is intentional.
4
Distinguish between homicide and limb retaliation
In limb retaliation, equality in the specific severed part is required (e.g., a healthy limb is not cut for a paralyzed limb). In homicide, such physical equality is not required; a healthy person can be killed for a chronically ill person.
This nuance is important for understanding the scope of equality.

بل هو بنده، والعاقل بالمجنون، والبالغ بالصبي، والصحيح بالأعمى والزمن وناقص الأطراف، والرجل بالمرأة، والفرع بأصل لا بعكسه.

قوله : بل هو بنده؛ ويقتل المستأمن بالمستأمن قياساً؛ لوجود المساواة بينهما، قال في تكملة البحر: لا يقتل المستأمن بنده استحساناً؛ لوجود المبيح، والند بكسر النون: المثل والنظير، وقد وقع بعض النسخ: المثل، بدل: الند، والأظهر ما كتبناه كما لا يخفى.
قوله : والعاقل.... الخ؛ أي يقتل العاقل بالمجنون وأما العكس فلا، كذا في قاضي خان.
قوله : والزمن: بفتح الزاء المعجمة، وكسر الميم صفة مشبهة من الزمانة: وهي آفة في الحيوانات، وهي وما عطف عليها معطوفات على الأعمى.
قوله : لا يعكسه؛ أي لا يقتلُ الأصلُ بفرعه؛ لقوله : «لا يقاد الوالد بولده، ولا السيد بعبده»؛ ولأنَّ الوالد لا يقتل ولده غالباً؛ لوفور شفقته، فيكون ذلك شبهة في سقوط القصاص؛ ولأنَّ الأب لا يستحقُ العقوبة بولده؛ لأنَّه، لإحيائه فَمِنَ المحال أن يكون الولد سبباً لإفنائه.
Book of Crimes / Types of Killing and What Necessitates Retaliation and Testimony Upon It. Neither of them is killed for a musta'min. This is our position, contrary to al-Shafi'i: neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a musta'min, nor does it create a doubt. If it created a doubt, then retaliation between slaves among themselves would not proceed. The obligation of retaliation in limbs depends on equality in the severed part after equality in inviolability. For this reason, a healthy limb is not cut with a paralyzed one. In life, this condition is not required, so a healthy person is killed for a chronically ill or paralyzed person. There is no equality between the limbs of a free person and a slave except in inviolability, because the slave, in terms of soul, is a morally responsible human being created inviolable. He (the commentator) said: 'This' means killing a Muslim for a dhimmi according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, i.e., a Muslim is not killed for him, because 'Ali ibn Abi Talib - may Allah be pleased with him - narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever, nor a person of covenant during his covenant.' Our proof is what we have recited from the Book of Allah and what we have narrated from the Sunnah, for it covers it by its generality. It has been authentically reported from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Salamah and Muhammad ibn al-Munkadir that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was brought a Muslim man who had killed a covenanted dhimmi, and he ordered that his neck be struck, saying: 'I am the most entitled of those who fulfill his covenant.' Retaliation depends on inviolability as we explained regarding the slave, and it is found considering the abode and moral responsibility. 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'They only paid the jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood and their wealth like our wealth,' meaning that they should be inviolable without doubt like a Muslim. He (the commentator) said: 'Neither of them is killed for a musta'min' means that neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a harbi who entered our abode with aman. Rather, he is killed for his equal: the sane for the insane, the adult for the child, the healthy for the blind, chronically ill, and one missing limbs, the man for the woman, and the branch for the root, not the opposite. He (the commentator) said: 'Rather, he is killed for his equal' means the musta'min is killed for his like, i.e., the musta'min, the sane for the insane, the adult for the child, the healthy for the blind, chronically ill, and one missing limbs, the man for the woman, and the branch for the root, not the opposite. With aman, his blood is not protected permanently as was mentioned, so equality is lacking, and likewise his disbelief incites war because he intends to return to his abode. He (the commentator) said: 'Rather, he is killed for his equal' and the musta'min is killed for the musta'min by qiyas due to the existence of equality between them. He said in Takmilat al-Bahr: The musta'min is not killed for his equal by istihsan due to the existence of the permissive factor. 'Al-nidd' with a kasra on the nun means the like and equivalent. Some copies have 'al-mithl' instead of 'al-nidd', and the apparent is what we wrote, as is not hidden. He (the commentator) said: 'The sane...' means the sane person is killed for the insane, but the opposite is not, as in Qadi Khan. He (the commentator) said: 'Al-zaman' with a fatha on the za and kasra on the mim is a descriptive adjective from zamana, which is a disease in animals, and what is coordinated with it are coordinated with al-a'ma (blind). He (the commentator) said: 'Not the opposite' means the root is not killed for its branch, due to his statement: 'The father is not retaliated for his son, nor the master for his slave.' And because the father usually does not kill his son due to abundant affection, so that is a doubt that causes retribution to fall. And because the father does not deserve punishment for his son, because of his bringing him into existence, so it is impossible for the son to be a cause of his destruction.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Crimes (Kitab al-Jinayat) discusses the conditions of equality required for retaliation (qisas) in homicide. The author begins by stating the Hanafi ruling that neither a Muslim nor a dhimmi is killed for a musta'min (a non-Muslim given temporary protection). This is contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that a Muslim can be killed for a dhimmi. The passage then provides proofs: 1) A hadith narrated by 'Ali ibn Abi Talib that the Prophet said, 'A Muslim is not killed for a disbeliever, nor a person of covenant during his covenant.' 2) An incident where the Prophet ordered a Muslim who killed a dhimmi to be executed. The underlying principle is that qisas depends on equality in inviolability (ismah)—the protected status of life. Since the dhimmi and Muslim share this inviolability (by virtue of paying jizyah and being under Islamic rule), qisas would apply if the Muslim kills a dhimmi? Actually the text says the opposite: Muslim is not killed for dhimmi. Wait: The ruling is clear: لا يقتل مسلم بكافر. So the hadith supports that. The Hanafi position uses the general proofs from Quran and Sunnah but also distinguishes between the inviolability of Muslim and dhimmi. There is also a discussion about the musta'min: his blood is not permanently protected, so he is not equal to a Muslim or dhimmi. Thus, a Muslim or dhimmi is not killed for a musta'min, but a musta'min is killed for his equal (another musta'min) by qiyas, though some scholars (as in Takmilat al-Bahr) prefer istihsan and say he is not killed even for his equal. The passage then lists other conditions of equality: a sane person is killed for an insane person, an adult for a child, a healthy person for a blind, chronically ill, or one missing limbs. These show that in homicide, physical completeness is not required; only inviolability matters. However, for limb retaliation (qisas in limbs), the text notes that equality in the specific severed part is required. For example, a healthy limb is not cut for a paralyzed limb. The passage concludes with the rule that a parent is not killed for a child (nor a master for a slave) due to the hadith and because of presumption of affection, which creates a doubt preventing qisas.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta’min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim territory under temporary protection (aman).
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt that prevents application of fixed punishments (hudud) or qisas.
الند (al-nidd): The equal or counterpart.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because all lives are equal.
B) No, because of the hadith and the doubt of affection.
C) Yes, but only if the child was an adult.
D) No, because the child's life is less valuable.

Key Takeaway
Equality for qisas requires inviolability, not physical perfection; exceptions include the musta'min (by istihsan) and the parent-child relationship.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
12 min
Equality Conditions in Qisas and Exceptions for Father, Master, and Mukatab
Ruling on equality condition in qisas and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of equality (mumathalah) required for qisas in Hanafi fiqh, identify the exceptions for father, master, and mukatab, and apply the principle of daf' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.

Core Idea
Qisas requires perfect equality; father, master, and ambiguity of status avert retaliation.

Qisas (retaliation) in Hanafi fiqh is not applied automatically; it is conditioned on equality between the killer and victim in certain attributes. Even when equality exists, exceptions arise due to relationships (father-son, master-slave) or legal ambiguity (mukatab). This lesson unpacks these rules and their proofs, showing how the principle of 'daf' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' averts qisas in doubtful cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of mumathalah
List the pairs specified in the matn: sane vs insane, adult vs child, healthy vs blind/chronically ill/amputee, man vs woman, descendant vs ancestor (not reverse). Understand that equality is required for qisas to apply.
2
Recognize the exceptions for father and master
Note that a father is not killed for his son, nor a master for his slave, based on the hadith. Understand the rationale: natural compassion (shafaqa) and the fact that the father is the cause of the son's existence.
This exception extends to mudabbar, mukatab, and partially-owned slaves.
3
Examine the special case of the mukatab
Analyze the four scenarios of a mukatab who commits murder and leaves wealth. Understand the dispute: when wealth suffices and no heir other than master, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold qisas is wajib, but Imam Muhammad (in one narration) says no qisas due to ambiguity over the mukatab's status at death.
The disagreement stems from whether the mukatab died free or slave, creating shubha.
4
Analyze the proofs and principles
The hadith 'لا يقاد الوالد بولده، ولا السيد بعبده' is the primary dalil. Also, rational arguments based on shubha and the maxim 'al-hudud tuqam bi al-shubuhat' (penalties are averted by doubts).
Qisas is considered a right of the heir, but it is averted if there is any ambiguity.
5
Determine the operative ruling
The mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice is to avert qisas in cases of shubha, following Imam Muhammad's view for the mukatab scenario. For father and master, the hadith is clear and no qisas is applied; instead, diyah or compensation is due.
The principle of daf' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat governs the mukatab case.

بل هو بنده، والعاقل بالمجنون، والبالغ بالصبي، والصحيح بالأعمى والزمن وناقص الأطراف، والرجل بالمرأة، والفرع بأصل لا بعكسه. بل هو بنده: أي يقتل المستأمن بمثله، وهو المستأمن، والعاقل بالمجنون، والبالغ بالصبي، والصحيح بالأعمى والزمن وناقص الأطراف، والرجل بالمرأة، والفرع بأصل لا بعكسه. بأمان، دمه ليس بمحقون على التأبيد كما مر، فانعدمت المساواة، وكذا كفره باعث على الحرب لقصده الرجوع إلى داره. قوله: بل هو بنده؛ ويقتل المستأمن بالمستأمن قياساً؛ لوجود المساواة بينهما، قال في تكملة البحر: لا يقتل المستأمن بنده استحساناً؛ لوجود المبيح، والند بكسر النون: المثل والنظير، وقد وقع بعض النسخ: المثل، بدل: الند، والأظهر ما كتبناه كما لا يخفى. قوله: والعاقل... الخ؛ أي بقتل العاقل بالمجنون وأما العكس فلا، كذا في قاضي خان. قوله: والزمن: بفتح الزاء المعجمة، وكسر الميم صفة مشبهة من الزمانة: وهي آفة في الحيوانات، وهي وما عطف عليها معطوفات على الأعمى. قوله: لا يعكسه؛ أي لا يقتل الأصلُ بفرعه؛ لقوله: «لا يقاد الوالد بولده، ولا السيد بعبده»؛ ولأنّ الوالد لا يقتل ولده غالباً؛ لوفور شفقته، فيكون ذلك شبهة في سقوط القصاص؛ ولأنَّ الأب لا يستحقُ العقوبة بولده؛ لأنَّه، لإحيائه فَمِنَ المحال أن يكون الولد سبباً لإفنائه. وبهذا لا يقتله إذا وجده في صف المشركين مقاتلاً أو زانياً وهو محصن؛ وهذا لأنَّ القصاص يستحقه الوارث بسبب انعقد للميت خلافه، ولو قتل به كان القاتل هو الابن نيابة. ولا سيد بعبده ومدبره ومكاتبه، وعبد ولده، وعبد بعضه له، ولا بعبدِ الرَّهنِ حتى يجتمع عاقداه وبمكاتب قتل عمداً عن وفاء، ووارث وسيد وإن اجتمعا ولا سيد بعبده ومدبّره، ومكاتبه، وعبد ولده، وعبد بعضه له، ولا بعبد الرهن حتى يجتمع عاقداه؛ لأنَّ المرتهن لا ملك له فلا يليه، والرَّاهن لو تولاه لبطل حق المرتهن في الدين، فيشترط إجماعهما؛ ليسقط حق المرتهن برضاه. (ولا بمكاتب) قتل عمداً عن وفاء، ووارث وسيد وإن اجتمعا؛ لأَنَّهُ ظهر الاختلاف بين الصحابة رضي الله عنهم في موته حراً أو رقيقاً، فإن مات حُرَّاً فالوليُّ هو الوارث، وإن مات رقيقاً فالولي المولى، فاشتبه من له الحق فلا يقتص قاتله وإن اجتمع الوارث والمولى وطولب بالفرق بين هذا وبين من زنى بابنته وهو محصن، فإنّه يرجم، أجيب بأنّ الرجم حق الله على الخصوص بخلاف القصاص فإن قيل: فيجب أن يحد إذا زنى بجارية ابنه أجيب بأنّ له حق الملك لقوله: «أنت ومالك لأبيك». قوله: ولا بعبد الرهن؛ يعني إذا قتل عبد الرهن غير العاقدين ليس للراهن قتل قاتله إلا برضاء المرتهن. قوله: ولا بمكاتب... الخ؛ وقد صُوِّرت هذه المسألة بأربع صور: الأولى: أنه قتل عمداً وترك مالاً يفي ببدل الكتابة ووارثاً حراً وسيداً، فحكمها عدم القصاص عند الجميع، وإن اتفق الوارث والسيّد على القصاص بناءً على أنه مما يندرء بالشبهات. والثانية: إنّه ترك وفاء ولم يترك وارثاً سوى سيّده، فحكمها القصاص عندهما، بناءً على تيقن حق الاستيفاء للمولى، وقال محمد رحمه الله: لا أرى فيها قصاصاً؛ لاشتباه سبب استيفائه، فإنه الولاء إن مات حراً والملك إن مات عبداً، فاندرأ به. والثالثة: أنه لم يترك وفاء، وله ورثة أحرار، فحكمُها وجوب القصاص للمولى عندهم جميعاً؛ لأنه عبد لانفساخ الكتابة بالموت لا عن وفاء، وقد أورد المصنف هنا.
Rather, he is his equal: the sane person [kills] the insane, the adult [kills] the child, the healthy [kills] the blind, the chronically ill, and those with missing limbs, the man [kills] the woman, and the descendant [kills] the ancestor, not the reverse. Rather, he is his equal: meaning the musta'min is killed for his like, which is the musta'min, the sane for the insane, the adult for the child, the healthy for the blind, the chronically ill, and those with missing limbs, the man for the woman, and the descendant for the ancestor, not the reverse. [The musta'min is under] amān, his blood is not inviolable perpetually as has passed, so equality is negated; similarly his disbelief incites war due to his intention to return to his abode. His statement: 'Rather, he is his equal': the musta'min is killed for the musta'min by analogy due to the existence of equality between them. In Takmilat al-Bahr it is said: the musta'min is not killed for his equal by istihsān due to the existence of the permissibility [of killing]. 'al-Nadd' with kasra on the nūn means 'like' and 'equivalent'. Some copies have 'al-mithl' instead of 'al-nadd', but what we wrote is more apparent as is not hidden. His statement: 'and the sane... etc.' meaning the sane is killed for the insane, but not the reverse, as in Qādī Khān. His statement: 'al-zamin': with fat-ha on the zā' and kasra on the mīm, a descriptive adjective from 'zamānah' which is a defect in animals, and what is coordinated with it is coordinated on 'al-a'mā'. His statement: 'not the reverse' meaning the ancestor is not killed for his descendant because of his statement: 'the father is not subject to qisās for his son, nor the master for his slave' and because the father usually does not kill his son due to the abundance of his compassion, so that becomes a doubt (shubha) for dropping qisās; and because the father does not deserve punishment through his son because he is the cause of his existence, so it is impossible that the son be a cause of his annihilation. And thus he is not killed if he finds him in the ranks of the polytheists as a fighter or an adulterer while he is muhsan; and this is because qisās is deserved by the heir due to the death of the deceased contrary to him, and if he were killed by him, the killer would be the son by proxy. 'Nor the master for his slave, his mudabbar, his mukātab, or the slave of his son, or the slave partially owned by him; nor for the mortgaged slave until both parties (the pawner and the mortgagee) agree; and [nor] for a mukātab who commits intentional murder and leaves enough to pay the kitābah, [if there is] an heir and the master even if they both [demand qisās]. Nor the master for his slave, his mudabbar, his mukātab, the slave of his son, the slave partially owned by him; nor for the mortgaged slave until both parties agree because the mortgagee has no ownership so he cannot administer [the qisās], and if the pawner were to administer it, the right of the mortgagee would be nullified, so the agreement of both is required to drop the mortgagee's right by his consent. 'Nor for a mukātab' who commits intentional murder and leaves enough [to pay the kitābah], [even if] the heir and master both [demand qisās] because there arose a difference among the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) regarding his death whether as free or slave: if he dies free, the guardian is the heir; if he dies slave, the guardian is the master. So the one with the right is ambiguous, so his killer is not subjected to qisās even if the heir and the master both agree. And if it is objected to differentiate between this and the case of one who commits adultery with his daughter while he is muhsan, for which he is stoned, the answer is that stoning is a right of Allah specifically, unlike qisās. If it is said: then he should be flogged if he commits adultery with his son's slave-girl, the answer is that he has the right of ownership due to his statement: 'You and your property belong to your father.' His statement: 'nor for a mortgaged slave' means if a mortgaged slave kills someone not being the two parties, the pawner cannot kill his killer except with the consent of the mortgagee. His statement: 'nor for a mukātab... etc.' This issue is depicted in four forms: First: he commits intentional murder and leaves wealth sufficient to pay the kitābah and a free heir and a master; its ruling is non-application of qisās according to all, even if the heir and master agree on qisās, because it is among matters that are averted by doubts. Second: he leaves sufficient wealth and leaves no heir other than his master; its ruling is qisās according to them both [Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad?] based on certainty of the right of execution for the master; and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: I do not see qisās in it due to the ambiguity of the cause of his execution, for it is [either] walā' if he dies free or ownership if he dies slave, so it is averted. Third: he does not leave sufficient wealth and has free heirs; its ruling is obligation of qisās for the master according to all of them because he is a slave due to the dissolution of the kitābah by death not from payment. And the author has mentioned [this].
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of qisās (retaliation) from the Hanafi school, specifically regarding the condition of equality between the killer and the victim, and exceptions where qisās is not applied. The matn (base text) states that qisās is established only when the killer and victim are equal in certain attributes: the sane for the insane, the adult for the child, the healthy for the blind, the chronically ill, and those with missing limbs, the man for the woman, and the descendant for the ancestor (but not the reverse). The commentator expands on each: the musta'min (foreigner with temporary protection) is killed for another musta'min by qiyās due to equality, but by istihsān he is not killed if he kills his equal because there is a legal basis for his killing (being non-Muslim). The sane is killed for the insane but not vice versa. The term 'zamin' (chronically ill) is defined. The exception for the ancestor (father) killing his descendant (son) is based on the hadith 'The father is not subjected to qisās for his son, nor the master for his slave.' The rationale is the father's natural compassion and that he is the cause of the son's existence. Therefore, qisās is not applied. Similarly, a master is not killed for his slave, including mudabbar, mukātab, or a slave partially owned. For a mortgaged slave (rahn), qisās requires the consent of both the pawner and mortgagee. The case of a mukātab (slave under contract of manumission) is detailed: if he commits murder and leaves enough to pay the kitābah, there is a dispute among the Companions about his status (free or slave) at death, so qisās is averted even if both heir and master agree. The commentary contrasts this with the case of adultery with one's daughter (stoning) where the right is Allah's, not the victim's. For adultery with a son's slave-girl, the father has ownership rights based on the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' so no punishment. The hashiya then details four scenarios for the mukātab: (1) leaves adequate wealth and has a free heir and master: no qisās by consensus; (2) leaves adequate wealth but no heir other than master: Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad (?) hold qisās, but Muhammad alone differs due to ambiguity; (3) leaves no wealth and has free heirs: qisās for master by consensus; (4) presumably the fourth scenario is not fully mentioned. The Hanafi position is that equality is required, and the exceptions are based on relationship and doubt (shubha). The relied-upon position is that qisās is not applied in cases of ambiguity.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim lands with temporary safe conduct (amān).
ند (nadd): An equal, like, or counterpart.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitābah) with his master, to pay a sum for freedom.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that averts the application of criminal penalties (especially hudud and qisas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling regarding qisas if a father kills his son?
A) The father is subject to qisas like any other killer.
B) The father is not subject to qisas due to the hadith 'لا يقاد الوالد بولده'.
C) The father is subject to qisas only if he kills his son without any reason.
D) The father may be killed for his son only if the son was a minor.

Key Takeaway
Qisas requires equality, but the father-son and master-slave relationships create a legal doubt (shubha) that averts retaliation; the mufta bihi is to apply the principle 'daf' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' in cases of ambiguity like the mukatab.

Qisas — Retaliation
beginner

No Qisas for Master over His Own Slave and the Special Case of the Mortgaged Slave
Ruling on lack of qisas for master over owned slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a master is not subject to qisas for killing his own slave, and explain the additional condition when the slave is mortgaged.

Core Idea
A master is not executed for killing his own slave because qisas requires equality in freedom; for a mortgaged slave, both mortgagor and mortgagee must consent.

This passage from Al-Wiqayah presents a clear ruling: the master of a slave is not subject to qisas (retaliatory execution) if he kills his own slave, regardless of the slave's status—regular, mudabbar, mukatab, etc. However, an exception arises when the slave is mortgaged: in that case, qisas can only be carried out if both the mortgagor (owner) and mortgagee (creditor) agree. Let us examine the legal reasoning behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a master killing his own slave. The ruling is that qisas is not imposed on the master.
The act itself is haram, but the penalty is financial, not retaliatory.
2
Note the categories of slaves covered
The text lists: regular slave (abd), mudabbar (whose emancipation is deferred after master's death), mukatab (under contract of manumission), slave of the master's child, a slave partially owned by the master, and mortgaged slave.
All these categories of slaves are owned in some capacity by the master.
3
Understand the special case of the mortgaged slave
If a mortgaged slave is killed by a third party (not the mortgagor or mortgagee), the right to execute the killer (qisas) is not absolute; both parties must consent.
The mortgagee's right over the slave's value as collateral requires his consent to waive that right.
4
Explore the reason for the mortgagee's involvement
The commentary explains that the mortgagee has a right to the slave's value (as collateral), so his consent is needed to ensure the debt is not compromised.
This prevents the mortgagor from undermining the mortgagee's security.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا سيد بعبده ومدبره ومكاتبه، وعبد ولده ، وعبد بعضه له ، ولا بعبدِ الرَّهنِ حتى يجتمع عاقداه [1] قوله : ولا بعبد الرهن ; يعني إذا قتل عبد الرهن غير العاقدين ليس للراهن قتل قاتله إلا برضاء المرتهن.
Nor a master for his slave, his mudabbar, his mukatab, the slave of his child, a slave partially owned by him, nor for a mortgaged slave until both parties agree. [1] His statement: 'Nor for a mortgaged slave' means if the mortgaged slave is killed by someone other than the two parties, the mortgagor cannot execute the killer except with the consent of the mortgagee.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: no qiṣāṣ (retaliation) is imposed on a master for killing his own slave, mudabbar (slave whose emancipation is deferred after death), mukātab (slave under contract of manumission), the slave of his child, a slave partly owned by him, or a mortgaged slave—except under the condition that both the mortgagor and mortgagee agree to the qiṣāṣ. The reason given in the commentary for the mortgagee's involvement is that the mortgagee has no ownership rights, so he cannot initiate qiṣāṣ; if the mortgagor alone could, he might undermine the mortgagee's right to the debt. Therefore, both must consent so that the mortgagee's right is waived by his own agreement.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment equal to the crime, specifically execution for intentional homicide.
الرَّهن (al-rahn): Mortgage or pledge; the slave is given as collateral for a debt.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is deferred until after the master's death.
المكاتب (al-mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission with the master, paying in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Regular slave
B) Mudabbar
C) Mortgaged slave
D) Mukatab

Key Takeaway
The master's ownership of the slave blocks qisas, but when the slave is mortgaged, the mortgagee's right requires consent for qisas against a third-party killer.

Four Scenarios of Qisas for Killing a Mukatab
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qiṣāṣ for killing a mukātab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of qisas for a mukatab and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute in the second scenario.

Core Idea
A mukātab's status at death determines qiṣāṣ: property or no property, free heir or master alone.

The passage examines the conditions under which qisas applies when a mukatab commits intentional murder. Four distinct scenarios arise based on whether the mukatab leaves property to pay the kitabah and whether he has free heirs besides his master. In one scenario, the Hanafi masters disagree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Read the passage and list each scenario with its key conditions: whether the mukatab leaves property covering the kitabah, and whether he has a free heir other than the master.
The scenarios are explicitly described; focus on the conditions.
2
Determine the status of the mukatab at death
For each scenario, deduce whether the mukatab dies free or slave. This depends on whether the kitabah is considered paid (if property covers it) or cancelled (if no property).
The status affects who holds the right to qisas.
3
Apply the rule of qisas
If the mukatab dies a slave, the master (as owner) has the right to qisas. If he dies free, the free heir has the right. If there is ambiguity, the ruling may differ.
Qisas is repelled by doubts (shubhah).
4
Note the ikhtilaf in the second scenario
In scenario 2, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule qisas for the master; Imam Muhammad disagrees. Identify the basis of each view: certainty vs. ambiguity.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
5
Identify the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Note the authority and practical consequence.
This determines operative law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبمكاتب قتل عمداً عن وفاء ووارث وسيد وإن اجتمعا [2] قوله : ولا بمكاتب... الخ; وقد صورت هذه المسألة بأربع صور : الأولى : أنه قتل عمداً وترك مالاً يفي ببدل الكتابة ووارثاً حراً وسيداً، فحكمها عدم القصاص عند الجميع، وإن اتفق الوارث والسيّد على القصاص بناءً على أنه مما يندرء بالشبهات. والثانية : إنّه ترك وفاء ولم يترك وارثاً سوى سيّده، فحكمها القصاص عندهما، بناءً على تيقن حق الاستيفاء للمولى، وقال محمد : لا أرى فيها قصاصاً؛ لاشتباه سبب استيفائه، فإنه الولاء إن مات حراً والملك إن مات عبداً ، فاندرأ به. والثالثة : أنه لم يترك وفاء ، وله ورثة أحرار، فحكمُها وجوب القصاص للمولى عندهم جميعاً ؛ لأنه عبد لانفساخ الكتابة بالموت لا عن وفاء. والرابعة التي هي أنه ما ترك وفاء ولا وارثاً سوى المولى أصلاً ، أو ترك ورثةً أرقاء، فإنّ حكمها يفهم بطريق الأولوية من الثالثة. فإنّه لما كان مجرد عدم ترك الوفاء مع وجودِ وارث آخر سبباً لانفساخ الكتابة ووجوب القصاص للمولى فيها ، فعند عدم الوارث سواه أولى. كذا فهم من تقرير الأكمله. هكذا في الجلبيه.
Nor for a mukātab who committed intentional murder and leaves property to cover the kitābah, even if the heir and master both demand it. [2] His statement: 'Nor for a mukātab... etc.' This issue has been described in four forms: First: He killed intentionally and left property covering the kitābah and a free heir and a master. Its ruling is no qiṣāṣ according to all, even if the heir and master agree, because it is repelled by doubts. Second: He left property covering the kitābah and no heir other than his master. Its ruling is qiṣāṣ according to the two (Abū Ḥanīfah and Abū Yūsuf), based on certainty of the master's right to execute, but Muḥammad said: I see no qiṣāṣ in it due to ambiguity of the cause of execution—it is walā' if he dies free and ownership if he dies a slave—so it is repelled. Third: He left no property covering the kitābah and has free heirs. Its ruling is obligation of qiṣāṣ for the master according to all, because he is a slave since the kitābah is dissolved by death without payment. Fourth: He left no property covering the kitābah and no heir other than the master, or he left slave heirs. Its ruling is understood by priority from the third. For if mere lack of property with another heir causes dissolution of the kitābah and obligation of qiṣāṣ for the master, then when there is no heir other than him, it is even more so. This is understood from the report of al-Akmalah. Thus in al-Jalbiyyah.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the ruling for a mukātab (a slave under contract of manumission) who commits intentional murder. The matn states that the master is not subject to qiṣāṣ if the mukātab leaves property to pay the kitābah (manumission price) and has a free heir, but the commentary describes four scenarios. In the first scenario, the mukātab leaves sufficient property and a free heir; no qiṣāṣ is imposed by consensus, even if the master and heir agree, because the situation is ambiguous (shubhah). In the second, he leaves property but no heir other than his master; Abū Ḥanīfah and Abū Yūsuf rule qiṣāṣ because the master's right is certain, but Muḥammad al-Shaybānī disagrees, arguing that if the mukātab dies free (because property covers kitābah), the heir is the free heir (the master only through walā'), and if he dies a slave, the master is the owner; thus the grounds for execution are ambiguous, so qiṣāṣ is dropped. In the third, he leaves no property but has free heirs; since the kitābah dissolves without payment, he is a slave at death, so the master has clear right to qiṣāṣ, and all agree. The fourth scenario (no property, no heir except master or slave heirs) follows by stronger reason (awlā) from the third: since even with a free heir, qiṣāṣ is given, without any heir it is even more so. This is an intra-Hanafī dispute: Muḥammad differs in the second image. The relied-upon position (al-muftā bihī) is that of Abū Ḥanīfah and Abū Yūsuf, as the text indicates by stating their view first.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitābah) to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
الوفاء (al-wafā'): The property left by the mukātab sufficient to pay the entire kitābah.
الولاء (al-walā'): Patronage right of the master over a freed slave; it determines inheritance and other rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second scenario of the mukātab, why does Imām Muḥammad reject qiṣāṣ?
A) Because the master has no right at all
B) Because the mukātab is considered free upon leaving property
C) Because the basis for execution is ambiguous (shubhah)
D) Because the heir is the master only

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies qiṣāṣ for killing a mukātab in most scenarios, but in the ambiguous second scenario, the relied-upon view is that qiṣāṣ is applied.

Qisas Dropped for Father and Execution Only by Sword
Ruling on paternal immunity and sword-only execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas against a father and explain the Hanafi position on the method of qisas execution, contrasting it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
A father is never executed for killing his son; qisas is always by sword, not by the killer's method.

This passage addresses two critical rules in Hanafi qisas law. First, the sanctity of fatherhood completely drops the right to qisas when the killer is the victim's father. Second, the actual execution of qisas is limited to the sword, even if the killer used a different method. This second rule is disputed with the Shafi'i school, which holds that the killer should be executed in the same manner he killed. Let's work through these rules and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and identify the two legal rulings it presents: one about the father's liability for qisas, and one about the method of execution.
The passage begins with 'wāyasquṭu qawadu warathi 'alā abīhi' and later 'wa lā yuqādu illā bis sayfi'.
2
Analyze the father immunity ruling
Understand why a father is not executed for killing his son. This is based on the sanctity of fatherhood (ḥurmah al-ubuwwah) and a Prophetic hadith. Note that qisas is completely dropped, not just postponed.
The text says 'li ḥurmati al-ubuwwah'.
3
Examine the method of execution dispute
The Hanafi school mandates execution by sword only. The Shafi'i school argues for like-for-like (killing the murderer by the same method). Identify the proofs each school uses.
The Hanafi proof is 'taḥqīqan li t-taswiya' (achieving equality).
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response and practical consequence
The Hanafi response accepts that if the killer's method works and kills him, it is fine; otherwise, the neck is severed. This shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): Hanafis always use sword, Shafi'is may use the same method.
The text: 'fa in māta fīhā, wa illā tujazzu raqabatuhu'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسقط قودٌ ورثه على أبيه : أي إذا قتل الأب شخصاً، وولي القصاص ابن القاتل يسقط القصاص لحرمة الأبوة. (ولا يقادُ إلا بالسيف)، هذا عندنا وعند الشَّافعي: يفعل به مثل ما فعل، فإن مات فيها ، وإلا تجز رقبته تحقيقاً للتّسوية. هذه الثلاثة على الترتيب، [3] قوله : فإن مات فيها ; أي إن مات بمثل ما فعلَ فقد تم الأمر، وإن لم يمت تجز رقبته ; أي يقطع عنقه.
And the qiṣāṣ of his heir is dropped against his father: meaning if the father kills a person, and the executor of qiṣāṣ is the son of the killer, the qiṣāṣ is dropped due to the sanctity of fatherhood. (And qiṣāṣ is only carried out with a sword), this is according to us (Hanafīs); according to al-Shāfi'ī: one does to him as he did, if he dies thereby, otherwise his neck is severed to achieve equality. These three are in order. [3] His statement: 'if he dies thereby' means if he dies by the like of what he did, the matter is complete; if he does not die, his neck is cut, meaning his throat is severed.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage addresses two distinct rules. First, if a father kills his son, the son's right to qiṣāṣ is dropped because of the sanctity of fatherhood (ḥurmah al-ubuwwah). Even though the son would normally be the heir entitled to demand qiṣāṣ, he cannot execute his father. This rule is based on a Prophetic tradition: 'A father is not executed for his son.' Second, qiṣāṣ must be carried out with a sword, not with the same method the killer used. The Hanafī position is that the killer is executed by sword regardless of how he killed. Imām al-Shāfi'ī, however, holds that the killer should be subjected to the same method of execution (e.g., if he killed by burning, he should be burned). The Hanafī response is that if the method causes death, it is acceptable; otherwise, the neck is severed to ensure equality. The text states 'these three are in order', referring to the three points: the father's immunity, the sword-only rule, and the Shāfi'ī view discussed. The commentary further clarifies that if the similar method fails to cause death, the neck is cut. This is an inter-madhhab dispute with the Shāfi'ī school.

Key Terms
حرمة الأبوة (ḥurmah al-ubuwwah): The sanctity/respect due to fatherhood that prevents qiṣāṣ against a father.
تجز رقبته (tujazzu raqabatuhu): His neck is severed; refers to execution by beheading with a sword.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafī ruling if a father kills his own son?
A) The son's heir can execute the father with a sword
B) Qiṣāṣ is dropped because of the sanctity of fatherhood
C) The father must pay blood money only
D) The father is executed but only if the son's family agrees

Key Takeaway
The sanctity of fatherhood completely bars qisas against a father, and when qisas is applicable, the Hanafi school mandates execution by sword alone, rejecting like-for-like methods.

Qisas of Mukatib Slave with No Heir Except Master
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on master's right to qisas against his mukatib slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on qisas of a mukatib slave with no heir except the master according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
When a mukatib slave commits murder and has no heir except his master, the master may execute qisas — but only if no other heir exists or the heir cannot pay diyah.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in qisas: a mukatib slave kills someone and has no legal heir except his master. The Hanafi school is internally divided on whether the master can then execute the killer. This dispute turns on the nature of the master's inheritance rights and the effect of the kitabah contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The passage describes a mukatib slave who commits murder. Identify the heir situation: either no heir except the master, or an heir but no payment.
The slave is not fully free; his kitabah contract grants him partial capacity.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They hold that the master can execute qisas in both sub-scenarios: when there is no other heir, or when there is an heir but that heir cannot pay diyah.
3
State the opposing position of Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad holds that the master cannot execute qisas; instead, the slave's kitabah is rescinded and he returns to slavery.
This position is based on the master not being a true heir.
4
Analyze the basis of each position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider the master the sole heir in the absence of other successors. Muhammad views the master as not being an heir because the slave is his property.
The commentary uses 'لأنه متعين' (because he is the specific heir) for Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf, the master executes qisas; under Muhammad, the slave returns to slavery. The matn endorses the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf as the relied-upon position.
The mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم يدع وارثاً غيرَ سَيِّدِهِ أو تركه ولا وفاءً أقاد سيده
(فإن لم يدع وارثاً غيرَ سَيِّدِهِ أو تركه ولا وفاءً أقاد سيده) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له خلافاً لمحمد الله ، وإن لم يترك وفاء قادَ السَّيِّدُ أَيضاً ؛ لأنه متعين.

Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the qisas of a mukatib slave who has committed murder. The ruling states that if the slave has no heir except his master, or if he has an heir but no means to pay the diyah, the master can execute qisas. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding that the master cannot execute qisas in such cases. The commentary explains that if the slave leaves no payment at all, the master also executes qisas because he is the only one entitled. The dispute is narrowed to the case where there is an heir but no payment. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as the matn itself states it.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah to earn his freedom by paying installments.
سيد (sayyid): The master who owns the slave.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission between a master and a slave for a fixed payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas Only by the Sword: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the method of qisas execution and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Qisas execution is restricted to the sword alone, even if the murder was by another method.

The method of executing qisas (retaliatory execution) is a point of disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. This passage presents the Hanafi position that only the sword may be used, supported by a hadith and a practical argument, against the Shafi'i view of like-for-like retribution. Understanding this dispute clarifies how textual and rational proofs interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding the instrument of qisas. The text says 'ولا يقاد إلا بالسيف' – qisas is not executed except by the sword.
This is the rule regardless of how the murder was committed.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that the execution should replicate the method of the murder, as per the Qur'anic principle of 'like-for-like' (فمن اعتدى عليكم فاعتدوا عليه بمثل ما اعتدى عليكم). If the victim was drowned, the murderer should be drowned; if not, then beheaded.
The Shafi'i view aims at precise equivalence.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi rely on the hadith 'لا قود إلا بالسيف' (No qisas except by the sword). Although it is a mursal hadith (zanni al-thubut), its meaning (dalalah) is clear: the negative 'لا' restricts qisas to the sword.
The hadith is cited as a specific textual limitation.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi rational argument
The Hanafi add a rational point: if the murderer does not die from the same method (e.g., drowning), one would still need to sever the neck to kill him, so true equality is not achieved. The sword ensures certain death and avoids this problem.
This is a rebuttal to the claim that the Shafi'i method achieves equality.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute results in different execution methods: Hanafis always use the sword; Shafi'is attempt to match the murder method, with beheading as fallback.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school is the sword-only view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولا يقادُ إلا بالسيف)، هذا عندنا وعند الشَّافعي : يفعل به مثل ما فعل، فإن مات فيها ، وإلا تجز رقبته تحقيقاً للتّسوية.
ولنا : قوله : «لا قودَ إلا بالسيف) ، وأيضاً يحتمل أن لا يموت فيحتاج إلى جز الرقبة، فلا تسوية.
And qisas is not executed except by the sword. This is our view, and according to al-Shafi'i: one should do to him as he did, so if he dies by that [method], otherwise his neck is severed to achieve equality. Our proof: his statement: 'No qisas except by the sword,' and also it is possible that he may not die, thus needing to sever the neck, so there is no equality.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the method of executing qisas. The Hanafi ruling is that qisas is carried out only by the sword, regardless of how the murder was committed. The Shafi'i school holds that the execution should mirror the method of the crime (like-for-like). The Hanafi response cites the hadith 'لا قود إلا بالسيف' (No qisas except by the sword) and points out a practical problem: if the murderer does not die from the same method, beheading becomes necessary, thus true equality is not achieved—the sword ensures a certain death.

Key Terms
قود (qawd): Retaliatory execution for murder (qisas).
سيف (sayf): Sword, the only instrument permitted for executing qisas according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main hadith cited by the Hanafis to support qisas only by sword?
A) 'لا قود إلا بالسيف'
B) 'فمن اعتدى عليكم فاعتدوا عليه بمثل ما اعتدى عليكم'
C) 'النفس بالنفس'
D) 'العين بالعين'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts qisas execution to the sword based on a specific hadith and the rational argument that true equality is impossible by other methods.

Qisas — Retaliation

6 min
Authority of Guardians in Qisas for the Insane and Minors
Ruling on who executes qisas on behalf of a ma'tuh or sabi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the powers of the father, executor, and judge regarding qisas on behalf of a ma'tuh or sabi, and distinguish which actions each guardian can perform.

Core Idea
The father can execute qisas and compromise, but not pardon; the executor can only compromise; the judge has the father's powers.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the hierarchical authority of guardians when a mentally incapacitated person (ma'tuh) or minor (sabi) is a victim of qisas crimes. Understanding these roles is essential for correctly applying qisas rulings in cases involving wards with limited legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the guardians mentioned
The passage lists three types: father (ab), executor (wasi), and judge (qadi). Also note the ma'tuh and sabi as the victims.
2
Extract the powers of the father
The father can execute qisas for limb injury (qati' yad) and for murder of a relative (qatil qarib). He can also compromise (sulh) for diyah. He cannot pardon ('afw).
3
Extract the powers of the executor
The executor can only compromise; he has no authority to execute qisas or pardon.
4
Compare the minor to the insane
The minor (sabi) is like the ma'tuh, so the father has the same powers over the minor's qisas.
5
Note the judge's parity with the father
The judge (qadi) is like the father, holding full powers to execute qisas and compromise, but also not to pardon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقيد أبو المعتوه قاطع يده، وقاتل قريبه، ويصالح ولا يعفو، وللوصي الصلح فقط، والصبي كالمعتوه ، والقاضي كالأب، هو الصحيح
The father of an insane person can execute qisas against one who cuts his hand, or kills his relative; he may compromise [for diyah] but not pardon. The executor may only compromise. The minor is like the insane person. The judge is like the father. This is the correct [view].
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage enumerates the powers of guardians over qisas for a mentally incapacitated person (ma'tuh) and a minor (sabi). The father of the ma'tuh can execute qisas for bodily harm (cutting of hand) and murder of a relative (e.g., the ma'tuh's child). He can also agree to a financial settlement (sulh) but cannot pardon (afw) because that would nullify the right without compensation. The executor (wasi) can only compromise; he has no authority to execute qisas or pardon. A minor (sabi) is like the ma'tuh, so his father has the same powers. The judge (qadi) is like the father in this regard, having full authority. The commentary notes that this is the correct (sahih) position, differentiating it from a weak report attributed to Muhammad.

Key Terms
معتوه (ma'tuh): A person with an intellectual disability that impairs legal capacity but not to the extent of complete insanity.
ولي (wali): Guardian who has the authority over the person and property of the ward.
وصي (wasi): Executor appointed by the father or judge to manage the ward's affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What can the father of an insane person (ma'tuh) NOT do regarding qisas?
A) Execute qisas for limb injury
B) Compromise for diyah
C) Pardon the offender
D) Execute qisas for murder of the ma'tuh's relative

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qisas, the father (and judge) can execute qisas and compromise but cannot pardon; the executor can only compromise.

Major Co-Heir Executing Qisas Before Minor Reaches Puberty: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on immediate vs. delayed qisas execution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a major co-heir can execute qisas before a minor co-heir becomes major, and explain the practical difference between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A major co-heir may execute qisas immediately per Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn require waiting until the minor reaches puberty.

When a murder victim leaves both major and minor heirs, a critical question arises: can the major execute qisas before the minor reaches maturity? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Hanifa and his two companions differ on the authority (wilaya) over the joint right of qisas. Understanding the basis of each view clarifies when qisas can be carried out and when it must be deferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: can a major co-heir execute qisas before the minor reaches puberty?
2
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa's view: the major can execute qisas for both immediately.
The matn states 'this is according to Abu Hanifa'.
3
Identify the opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the major must wait until the minor becomes major.
They argue that qisas is a joint right.
4
Follow the opposing proof
The joint right argument: qisas is a shared right (haqq mushtarak), so all co-heirs must consent; the minor cannot consent, so execution must be delayed.
5
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the major's right is complete and not contingent on the minor's consent; the minor's share is deferred until majority.
If the major is a guardian, there is consensus allowing immediate execution.
6
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, qisas can be carried out immediately; under Sahibayn, it must be delayed until the minor becomes major.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستوفي الكبير قبل كِبَرِ الصغيرِ قَوَداً لهما، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وقالا : ليس للكبير ولاية القصاص حتى يدرك الصغير البلوغ ؛ لأنه حق مشترك
The major may execute qisas for both before the minor reaches maturity. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The major has no authority of qisas until the minor reaches puberty, because it is a joint right.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa holds that when a murder victim leaves both a major and a minor heir, the major can execute qisas even before the minor becomes an adult. The two companions (Sahibayn) disagree: the major cannot execute qisas until the minor reaches puberty, because the right to qisas is shared. However, the commentary notes that if the major is a guardian of the minor (e.g., father or grandfather), he can execute by consensus. The ikhtilaf is only when the major is not a guardian. The matn states Abu Hanifa's view, indicating it is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
كبير (kabir): An adult who has reached legal majority (puberty).
صغير (saghir): A minor who has not reached puberty.
ولاية القصاص (wilayat al-qisas): The authority to execute or pardon qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when can a major co-heir execute qisas?
A) Immediately after the murder
B) Only after the minor co-heir reaches puberty
C) Only if the major is a guardian of the minor
D) Never, the qisas must be waived

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi position is that a major co-heir may execute qisas even before a minor co-heir reaches puberty, unless the major is a guardian, in which case there is consensus.
