Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Obligation of Qisas and Settlement by Heirs
Ruling on immediate qisas, settlement by one heir, and case of free and slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qisas is due immediately, apply the principle of indivisibility to a scenario where one heir pardons, and explain the evidence for spouses inheriting qisas.

Core Idea
Qisas is indivisible: one heir's pardon or settlement waives it for all, and it is due immediately.

When a murder occurs, the victim's heirs have a right to demand qisas (retribution) or accept blood money. This lesson explores three key rulings from the Hanafi school: first, that qisas is due immediately because it is a contractual obligation; second, that qisas is indivisible, so one heir's action affects all; and third, that spouses inherit this right, contrary to some other schools. We will also examine a case where a free man and a slave jointly kill and their representative settles for a lump sum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on immediacy
Locate the statement that qisas is obligatory immediately (ḥālan). Understand the reason: it is a financial obligation arising from a contract (ʿaqd), analogous to mahr or price, not like diya which is contractual.
This distinguishes qisas from diya regarding deferment.
2
Apply the indivisibility rule
Analyze the statement that each heir can execute, waive, or settle qisas, but if one heir's right drops, all rights drop because qisas is indivisible (lā yatajazzā). Work through a scenario: if one of three heirs pardons, what happens to the other two?
This is a key practical difference from diya, which is divisible.
3
Evaluate the spouses' dispute
Read the claim: Malik and al-Shafi'i exclude spouses from inheriting qisas; the Hanafi position includes them. Examine the Hanafi evidence: the hadith of the woman of Ashaym and the consensus that spouses inherit property. Assess the opponents' reasoning (inheritance by lineage, not cause).
The Hanafi response uses both naql (transmission) and ʿaql (reason).
4
Work through the joint killer settlement
Consider the case: a free man and a slave (with his master) instruct a representative to settle with the heirs for a fixed amount (1000 dirhams). Determine how the payment is split between them. The text says half each. Trace the analogy: it is like two men buying a slave equally – the price is divided equally because the substitute (badal) is for the victim's blood, not for the killers' differing values.
The objection based on khulʿ (divorce compensation) is rejected because there the substitute is for the wives' private parts, which differ in value.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence of the Hanafi position on spouses: a widow or widower can demand qisas or pardon, whereas according to Malik and al-Shafi'i they cannot. This affects the distribution of rights among heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب حالاً وبصلح أحدهم وبعفوه ، ولمن بقي حصته من الدية، فإن صالح بألف وكيل سيد عبد وحرّ قتلا فالصلح عن دمهما بألف بنصف) : أي إن كان القاتل حرَّاً وعبداً، فأمر الحر ومولى العبدِ رجلاً بأن يصالح من دمهما على ألف ففعل، فالألفُ على الحر والمولى نصفان. [ ١ ] قوله : ويجب حالاً ، لأنه مال واجب بالعقد، والأصل في أمثاله الحلول نحو المهر والثمن، بخلاف الدية ؛ لأنها ما وجبت بالعقد. [٢] قوله : حق جميع الورثة ؛ فكل منهم يتمكن من الاستيفاء والإسقاط عفواً وصلحاً، ومن ضرورة سقوط حق البعض في القصاص سقوط حق الباقين فيه ؛ لأنه لا يتجزأ. [٣] قوله : خلافا لمالك والشافعي في الزوجين ؛ لأنَّ الوراثة خلافة، وهي بالنسب دون السبب ؛ لانقطاعه بالموت، قلنا : إنّه فاسد بالنقل والعقل ؛ أما الأول فبحديث امرأة أشيم، وأما الثاني ؛ فلأنهما موروثان كسائر الأموال بالاتفاق، فيجب أن يكون في حق الزوجين كذلك. [٤]قوله : فإن صالح ؛ صورته : قتل الحر والعبد رجلاً، فأمر الحر ومولى العبد شخصاً بأن يصالح عن دم عليهما من ورثة المقتول على ألف درهم ففعل، فالألف على الحر ومولى العبد نصفان ؛ لأنَّ عقد الصلح أضيف إليهما ؛ لأنَّ الواجب بدل عن القصاص، والقصاص عليهما على السواء، فيقسَمُ البدل عليهما على السواء ؛ كرجلين اشتريا عبداً كان الثمن عليهما على السواء ؛ لأنَّ الثمن بدل العبد، وقد ملكاه على السواء، فبدله كذلك. فإن قيل : يجب أن يكون الألف عليهما على قدر قيمتهما ؛ لأنه بالصلح اشترى كل واحد منهما دمه المستحق بولي القتيل، كمن خالع امرأتيه على ألف درهم، يقسم الألف عليهما على قدر قيمة بضعهما. قلنا : الألف بدل دم المقتول، وهما في إتلافه على السواء، والمجامـع بـدل بضعهما، فيقسم على قيمة بضعهما. هكذا في «الكفاية»
And it is obligatory immediately, and one of them may settle or pardon, and for those who remain their share of the blood money. If he settled for a thousand, a representative of the master of a slave and a free man who killed, the settlement for their blood is for a thousand, half. That is, if the killer was a free man and a slave, and the free man and the slave's master instructed a man to settle for their blood for a thousand, and he did, then the thousand is half on the free man and half on the master. [1] He said: It is obligatory immediately, because it is wealth obligated by contract, and the principle in such cases is immediacy, like the dower and the price, unlike the blood money because it is not obligated by contract. [2] He said: It is the right of all the heirs; so each of them can execute and waive by pardon or settlement, and necessarily, the dropping of the right of some in qisas drops the right of the rest in it, because it is indivisible. [3] He said: Contrary to Malik and al-Shafi'i regarding the spouses, because inheritance is succession, and it is by lineage not by cause, because it terminates with death. We say: This is invalid by transmission and reason; as for the first, by the hadith of the woman of Ashaym; as for the second, because they (spouses) inherit like other property by consensus, so it must be in the case of spouses similarly. [4] He said: As for his settlement; its scenario: A free man and a slave killed a man, so the free man and the slave's master instructed a person to settle for the blood due from both of them with the heirs of the slain for a thousand dirhams, and he did, then the thousand is half on the free man and half on the master, because the contract of settlement was added to both of them, because the obligation is a substitute for qisas, and qisas is equally on both, so the substitute is divided equally between them, like two men who bought a slave; the price is equally on both because the price is a substitute for the slave, and they both owned him equally, so its substitute is likewise. If it is said: The thousand should be on them according to their value, because by settlement each one bought his own blood that is due to the heir of the slain, like a man who divorces his two wives for a thousand dirhams, the thousand is divided between them according to the value of their private parts. We say: The thousand is a substitute for the blood of the slain, and they are equal in destroying it, and the total is a substitute for their private parts, so it is divided according to the value of their private parts. Thus in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Hidayah or similar work, concerning the obligation of qisas (retribution) and its settlement. The author explains several rulings: First, qisas is due immediately (حالاً) if no deferment is stated; this is because it is a financial obligation arising from contract, like mahr or price, unlike diya (blood money) which is not contractual. Second, each heir has the right to execute qisas, pardon, or settle; if one heir settles or pardons, qisas is waived for all because it is indivisible. Third, contrary to Malik and al-Shafi'i, spouses inherit qisas according to the Hanafi school; the proof is the hadith of the woman of Ashaym and the principle that spouses inherit property. Fourth, in the case where a free man and a slave jointly kill someone, and they settle through a representative for a fixed amount (1000 dirhams), the payment is divided equally between the free man and the slave's master, because qisas is equally due on both. An objection is raised: should the division be based on the value of each killer (since the free man's life is more valuable)? The reply is that the settlement is a substitute for the victim's blood, which is equal, not for the killers' lives. This is analogous to two men buying a slave equally. Thus, the payment is halved.

Key Terms
الوجوب حالاً (al-wujūb ḥālan): The obligation is immediate without deferment; used here for qisas payment upon settlement.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retribution in kind for murder or injury; the right of the victim's heirs to execute or waive.
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): Settlement or compromise; in this context, an agreement to accept blood money in lieu of qisas.
بدل (badal): Substitute or compensation; here, the blood money is a substitute for qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if one heir pardons the qisas, what happens to the rights of the other heirs?
A) The other heirs can still demand qisas
B) The other heirs lose their right to qisas because it is indivisible
C) The other heirs can demand blood money instead
D) The pardon is invalid without the consent of all heirs

Key Takeaway
Qisas is an immediate, indivisible right of all heirs; one heir's action in pardoning or settling terminates the qisas for all.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Qisas When Multiple Kill One or One Kills Multiple
Ruling on qisas for group killing and multiple victims with proof and rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a group kills one person and when one person kills a group, and explain the proof from the practice of Caliph Umar.

Core Idea
If a group kills one, all are executed; if one kills many, one execution suffices.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on qisas in cases of multiple killers or multiple victims. The key principle is that if there is cooperation (tamalu'), all participants are equally liable, and one execution suffices for all victims. This position is supported by the precedent of Caliph Umar and differs from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening line: 'A group is killed for a single person, and vice versa.' Understand that if multiple people kill one, all are executed; if one kills multiple, one execution suffices for all victims.
2
Note the condition about guardians
If only one guardian (heir) of the victim is present, qisas is carried out for that guardian and the rights of other heirs drop. This prevents sequential executions.
3
Consider the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that if order of killing is known, only the first killer is executed for the first victim; for the rest, blood money is due. If order is unknown, some say lots are cast.
This shows a clear difference in practical outcome.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school relies on the action of Caliph Umar, who executed seven men from San'a for killing one man. He said, 'If the people of San'a had cooperated against him, I would have killed them all.' This demonstrates the principle of tamalu' (cooperation).
The proof is an athar (companion practice), considered a sunna.
5
Understand the rebuttal of an objection
An objection is raised that division of compensation should be according to value (like in khul‘). The Hanafi response: the substitute here is for the victim's blood, which is equal for all killers, unlike the value of a wife's private parts.
This reinforces the equality of liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And a group is killed for one, and vice versa, sufficiently if their guardian is present. And if one guardian is present, he is killed for him, and the right of the rest drops. (And a group is killed for one, and vice versa, sufficiently if their guardian is present) means: One person is killed for a group, and his killing suffices, and there is nothing for their guardians other than that, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because according to him, he is killed for the first, and for the rest the wealth is due. And if the first is not known, he is killed for all of them, and the blood money is divided among them. And it is said: Lots are cast, and he is killed for the one whose lot appears. And if one guardian is present, he is killed for him, and the right of the rest drops: i.e., if one guardian is present, the qisas is carried out for him. And qisas is equally on both, so the substitute is divided equally between them, like two men who bought a slave; the price is equally on both because the price is a substitute for the slave, and they both owned him equally, so its substitute is likewise. If it is said: The thousand should be on them according to their value, because by settlement each one bought his own blood that is due to the heir of the slain, like a man who divorces his two wives for a thousand dirhams, the thousand is divided between them according to the value of their private parts. [1] He said: And a group is killed... etc; in al-Inayah: Analogy does not require it because equality is lacking, but it is abandoned due to what is narrated that seven men from San'a killed a man, and Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) judged qisas against them, and said: If the people of San'a had cooperated against him, I would have killed them all. al-Tamalu' is cooperation. Akhi Jalabi said: al-Zahidi said: All of them are subject to qisas only if from each
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on qisas when multiple people kill one person, or one person kills multiple people. The Hanafi position: If a group kills one, they are all executed, and one execution suffices for all; the heirs of additional victims get nothing extra. If only one heir of the victim is present, qisas is executed for that heir and the rights of other heirs drop. This is contrary to al-Shafi'i, who holds that if multiple kill one, only the first killer is executed for the first victim (if order is known); for the rest, blood money is due. If the order is unknown, some Shafi'is say lots are cast. The Hanafi proof includes the practice of Caliph Umar who executed seven people for killing one man, citing the principle of cooperation (tamalu'). The commentary also analogizes to two men buying a slave equally: the price is divided equally, just as here the substitute (qisas) is equal. An objection that division should be according to value (e.g., free vs. slave) is rebutted: the substitute is for the victim's blood, which is equal for all killers.

Key Terms
التمالأ (al-tamālu’): Cooperation or conspiracy in killing; here, if all cooperated, all are subject to qisas.
القصاص (al-qisās): Retaliation in kind, i.e., execution for murder, subject to conditions.
الدية (al-diya): Blood money paid as financial compensation for killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the one who struck the fatal blow
B) All three, because of cooperation
C) Only one, chosen by lot
D) None; blood money is paid instead

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, cooperation (tamalu') in killing makes all participants equally liable for qisas, overriding the default requirement of individual retaliation.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Qisas for a Group Killing an Individual
Establishes qisas for group killing one and vice versa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for a group that kills one person, identify the condition required for all to be executed, and explain the key difference with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A group can be executed for killing one person if each inflicted a fatal wound – equality is not required.

Can multiple people be executed for the death of a single victim? This passage from al-Wiqayah presents the Hanafi ruling that a group may indeed face qisas if each contributed a fatal wound. The ruling rests on a strong precedent from Caliph Umar, overriding the normal qiyas principle that would require equality between each wound. You will learn the exact conditions and how this differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the joint killing of one person by a group. The ruling is that qisas (retaliatory execution) is wajib upon the entire group, provided conditions are met.
The text also mentions the reverse case (one kills a group) but focuses on group killing one.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the narrated action of Caliph Umar, who executed seven men from San'a for killing one man. He stated he would kill all if the whole city conspired.
This is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) but considered strong enough to override qiyas.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit action of Umar shows that qisas applies collectively despite the lack of individual wound equality. The legal link is that each killer's participation was fatal in concert.
The text explicitly says the ruling abandons qiyas (equality) due to this precedent.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that qisas cannot apply to all because equality between wound and death is required; if order of blows is known, only the first killer is executed, others pay diyah.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different weighting of qiyas vs. naql.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school considers Umar's ruling as mufta bihi (operative) because it is a direct application of the Shariah by a companion. Al-Zahidi added a condition: each killer must have inflicted a wound capable of causing death.
Without that condition, no qisas – only diyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقتل جمع بفرد، وبالعكس اكتفاء إن حضر وليهم، وإن حضر واحد قتل له ، وسقط حق البقية
[1]
(1) ويقتل جمع بفرد... الخ؛ في «العناية: والقياس لا يقتضيه؛ لانتفاء المساواة، لكنّه ترك بما روي أنّ سبعة من أهل صنعاء قتلوا رجلاً، فقضى عمر الله بالقصاص عليهم ، وقال : لو تمالأ عليه أهل صنعاء لقتلتهم، التمالؤ: التعاون. قال أخي جلبي ) : قال الزاهدي : إنّما يقتص جميعهم إذا وجد من كل واحدٍ منهم جرح يصلح لزهوق الروح، فأما إذا كانوا نظارة مقرنين أو معينين بالإمساك والأخذ لا قصاص عليهم. انتهى.

(۳) «العناية»(١٠: ٢٤٣).

A group is killed for an individual, and vice versa, as sufficient if their guardian is present. If one guardian is present, qisas is carried out for him, and the right of the remaining guardians drops. (1) A group is killed for an individual... etc.; in al-`Inayah: Qiyas does not require it because equality is absent, but it is abandoned due to what is narrated: Seven people from San`a' killed a man, so `Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) ruled that they be executed in retaliation, and said: 'If all the people of San`a' had conspired against him, I would have killed them all.' Tamaalu' means cooperation. Akhi Chalabi said: al-Zahidi said: They are all executed in retaliation only if from each one there is a wound suitable for causing death. But if they were mere spectators, confederates, or helpers by holding and seizing, then no qisas on them. Ended.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage from the text al-Wiqayah states the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a group kills one person or one person kills a group, with the condition that the victim's guardian(s) are present. If all guardians are present, the entire group (or the single person) is executed. If only one guardian is present, the qisas is carried out for that guardian, and the other guardians lose their right to qisas. The commentary from al-`Inayah explains that this ruling goes against qiyas, which would require equality (each killer's wound must be exactly equal to the victim's), but it is upheld based on the historical precedent of Caliph `Umar, who executed seven men for killing one man, stating that he would have killed all if the whole city conspired. However, al-Zahidi adds a condition: qisas is only on all if each killer inflicted a wound capable of causing death; if they were merely helpers or spectators, qisas is not applicable. This ruling establishes the Hanafi position that collective responsibility for qisas is valid despite the lack of individual equality, contrary to the Shafi'i school which would require qisas only on the first killer and diyah for the others.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment equivalent to the harm caused, typically execution for murder.
ولي (wali): The legal guardian of the victim who has the right to demand qisas or accept diyah.
تمالؤ (tamaalu'): Concerted effort or conspiracy; cooperation in killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the Hanafi school, which condition must be met for a group of killers to be executed in qisas for killing one person?
A) All killers must have intended to kill.
B) Each killer must have inflicted a wound capable of causing death.
C) The victim must have no guardian.
D) The group must be from the same tribe.

Key Takeaway
A group can be executed for killing one person if each member inflicted a fatal wound, based on Umar's precedent, overriding the qiyas of equality.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Two Hands Not Cut for One Hand: Joint Liability and the Principle of Divisibility
Explains why two persons who cut one hand are liable for diyah, not qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint cutting of a hand and explain why qisas is not applied, using the principle of divisibility (tajazzi).

Core Idea
A hand is divisible; two cutters share diyah, not qisas.

This passage addresses a key distinction in Hanafi criminal law: when two people act together to cut one hand, they are not punished by amputation of their own hands. Instead, they owe blood money (diyah). The reasoning hinges on whether the body part is physically divisible. This lesson unpacks the proof and the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The matn states 'two hands are not cut for a hand.' This means that if two persons cut one hand, they are not both subject to qisas (amputation). Instead, they are jointly liable for diyah.
The title itself is the core ruling.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The commentary explains: 'the cutting occurred by their combined effort, and the place (hand) is divisible (mutajazzi'), so part of the act is attributed to each.' This distinguishes the hand from the soul, which is indivisible.
Divisibility is a key legal concept (al-mahal mutajazzi').
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes from the case of killing: if multiple killers execute one person, all are executed. Similarly, if multiple persons cut one hand, all should lose a hand in qisas.
This is a classic qiyas argument.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rebuts by emphasizing that the hand is divisible, so the liability can be apportioned. The soul is not divisible, so each killer is fully liable. Therefore, the analogy fails.
The distinction is based on the nature of the subject (mahal).
5
Apply the ruling to other scenarios
The text also discusses a case where one man cuts the right hands of two men. It explains that if only one victim's guardian is present to execute qisas, the other receives diyah. This shows the detailed application of the principle.
Also note the condition that passing the knife over the whole limb is required for qisas.

And two hands are not cut for a hand, even if two men pass a knife over a hand and it is cut, they are both liable for its diyah. And if a man cuts the right hands of two men, then they both have his right hand and the diyah of a hand; if one of them is present and cuts [his hand], then the other gets the diyah, and the right of the remaining is dropped according to us. (1) This is our position, and according to al-Shafi`i: if two men take a knife and pass it over the hand of another, both of their hands are cut by analogy to the soul. But our argument: the cutting occurred by their combined effort, and the place is divisible, so each is attributed part, unlike the soul, for the loss of life is indivisible. [2] His statement: 'its diyah'; meaning the diyah of the hand is half the diyah of the soul, so it is upon both, so each of them owes a quarter of the diyah of the soul from their own wealth. Thus in al-Tabyeen. [3] His statement: 'if two men take a knife'; meaning each from one side. But if one passed a knife from one side and the other from the other side until the two knives met in the middle and the hand was severed, no qisas is obligatory on either by consensus, because from each there was no passing of the weapon over part of the limb, and passing is a condition for qisas. Thus in al-Tabyeen; this is what Akhi Chalabi conveyed. [4] His statement: 'if a man cuts the right hands of two men'; he restricted it thus, because if he cuts the right of one and the left of the other, both of his hands are cut, and it is not said that equality is negated because he did not deprive each one of...
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi ruling on joint cutting of a hand and the case of one man cutting the right hands of two men. The matn states that two hands are not cut for one hand, meaning if two people actively cause the severance of one hand, they are not both subjected to qisas (cutting of their hands); instead, they are jointly liable for the diyah (blood money). The reason, as the commentary explains, is that the hand is physically divisible (al-mahal mutajazzi'), so the act of cutting can be attributed to each in part. This contrasts with killing, where the soul (ruh) is indivisible; therefore, in murder, all participants can be executed. The commentary also addresses a specific scenario: if a man cuts the right hands of two men, both victims become entitled to his right hand (for qisas) and the diyah of a hand. If only one victim's guardian is present to execute qisas, the other victim receives the diyah, and the rights of the remaining guardians (if any) are dropped. This is the Hanafi view, which differs from the Shafi'i school. Shafi'i analogizes from the case of killing, arguing that if two men cut one hand, both should lose a hand in qisas. The Hanafi rebuttal emphasizes the divisibility of the hand versus the indivisibility of life. The commentary further clarifies conditions for qisas: if each of the two men holds the knife from opposite sides and the hand is severed in the middle, there is no qisas by consensus because neither passed the knife over the whole limb. This passage reinforces the Hanafi principle that the application of qisas depends on direct causation and the nature of the body part.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Hand; in this context, the limb subject to qisas or diyah.
متجزي (mutajazzi'): Divisible; capable of being apportioned in parts.
دية (diyah): Blood money; monetary compensation for bodily harm or death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not require qisas on two persons who jointly cut one hand?
A) Because cutting a hand requires two hands as punishment
B) Because the hand is divisible, so each is only partly responsible
C) Because qisas is only for murder
D) Because the victims must both be present

Key Takeaway
When multiple persons jointly cut a divisible limb like a hand, they are liable for diyah, not qisas, because the injury can be apportioned.
